Let's think of a known problem: "Determine all k such that there exist n distinct lines covering all lattice points in a triangle." This might be a combinatorial geometry problem.

Alternate approach: Represent lines as sets of points. Condition that a line is sunny means it's not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So sunny lines have slope not 0, not infinite, not -1. So slopes can be any other real number. We can choose lines with any slopes; they can pass through many points. But we have a limited number of lines.

Observation: For any line that is not horizontal/vertical/slope -1, it can intersect the triangle in a line segment; the set of lattice points on it inside the triangle is limited. However, we can choose lines that are not through lattice points, but they might still contain lattice points if we choose them appropriately. For example, we can choose a line through (1,1) and (2,2) (slope 1) which contains many lattice points if we consider the whole line, but within the triangle, only points with sum ≤ n+1 and coordinates positive. For slope 1, the line y=x passes through (1,1),(2,2),...,(n,n) but (n,n) sum=2n > n+1 for n≥2, so only points with sum ≤ n+1: those with x ≤ (n+1)/2. So for n=4, it contains (1,1) and (2,2). That's only 2 points. So not many.

Similarly, a line with slope 2: through (1,2) and (2,4) but (2,4) sum=6>5, not needed; (1,2) only? Actually (1,2) sum=3, (2,4) sum=6>5, so only (1,2). So not many. So to cover many points, lines must have slope that allows many lattice points within the triangle. But we have many points; each line can cover at most something like n points if it is a line that passes through many lattice points. For instance, the line y = 2x? For n=4, points: (1,2) and (2,4) not allowed; (3,6) no; so only 1. The line y = x/2? Through (2,1) and (4,2) but (4,2) sum=6>5; so only (2,1). So not many. The line x+y = d: that's slope -1; we already know that covers d-1 points (since a,b≥1, a+b=d gives a from 1 to d-1, so d-1 points). So for d=n+1, it covers n points. So the only line that can cover n points from our set is the hypotenuse line (since it's parallel to x+y=0). Horizontal lines cover n points? For y=c, points with a from 1 to n+1-c, so number of points = n+1-c. For c=1, that's n; for c=2, that's n-1; etc. So horizontal line y=1 covers n points (since a from 1 to n, because n+1-1=n). Similarly, vertical line x=1 covers n points (b from 1 to n). So lines parallel to x-axis or y-axis can cover up to n points. So non-sunny lines (horizontal/vertical) can cover many points. Lines parallel to x+y=0 can cover up to n points if d=n+1. So the only lines that can cover n points are those that are either horizontal (y=1), vertical (x=1), or slope -1 (x+y=n+1). But these are exactly the non-sunny lines. So sunny lines cannot cover n points; they can cover at most n-1? Possibly they can cover n points if they are not parallel to axes or slope -1 but still pass through many lattice points within the triangle. But consider a line with slope 1/2: does it pass through many lattice points? For example, line through (2,1) and (4,2): slope 1/2, passes through (2,1),(4,2) but also (6,3) etc. Within triangle of n=4, points: (2,1) sum=3; (4,2) sum=6>5; so only (2,1) maybe. Another: line through (1,2) and (3,1): slope -1/2, that's slope -1/2; points: (1,2) sum=3; (3,1) sum=4; (5,0) no; so only two points. So indeed, a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1 can contain at most 2 points from the set? Not necessarily; consider a line through (1,1) and (3,2): slope 1/2, points: (1,1) sum=2; (3,2) sum=5; (5,3) sum=8 >5; so only 2. Another: line through (1,1) and (4,3): slope 2/3, points: (1,1) sum=2; (4,3) sum=7>5; so only 1. So it seems that any line that is not horizontal/vertical/slope -1 can contain at most 2 points from the triangle (since the points are in a convex set with a boundary that is not parallel to the line; the line can intersect the triangle in a segment, but the lattice points on that segment are limited because the triangle is small). But is it possible to have a line that contains 3 points? For example, consider n=5, triangle: a+b ≤6. Points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1). Could there be a line with slope 1/2 that goes through (1,1),(2,2)? That's slope 1, not 1/2. To have slope 1/2, points like (1,1) and (3,2): slope (2-1)/(3-1)=1/2; (1,1) sum=2; (3,2) sum=5; (5,3) sum=8 >6? Actually 5+3=8>6, so only two. Could have (2,1) and (4,2): slope 1/2, (2,1) sum=3; (4,2) sum=6; (6,3) sum=9>6; so two. Could have (1,2) and (3,3): slope 1/2? (3-2)/(3-1)=1/2; (1,2) sum=3; (3,3) sum=6; (5,4) sum=9>6; so two. So seems only two. Could have a line with slope -2 that goes through (1,4) and (2,2): slope (2-4)/(2-1)=-2; (1,4) sum=5; (2,2) sum=4; (3,0) not; so two. Possibly a line with slope 1/3 through (1,1) and (4,2): slope 1/3, points: (1,1) sum=2; (4,2) sum=6; (7,3) sum=10>6; so two. So it appears that for any line not horizontal/vertical/slope -1, it can contain at most 2 points from the set of points with a+b ≤ n+1. But is that always true? Consider a line with slope 2 that passes through (1,2) and (2,4): (2,4) sum=6>5 for n=4; for n=5, (1,2) sum=3; (2,4) sum=6; (3,6) sum=9>6; so only two. For n=6, triangle: a+b ≤7. Points: (1,1)...(6,1) etc. Could a line with slope 2 go through (1,2) and (3,4)? (1,2) sum=3; (3,4) sum=7; (5,6) sum=11>7; so two. Could it go through (2,1) and (4,3)? slope (3-1)/(4-2)=1; no. So it seems the maximum number of lattice points on a line inside a right triangle with legs along axes and hypotenuse of slope -1 is limited. In general, if a line has slope m, then the lattice points on it are of the form (x0 + t, y0 + mt) for integers t, if m is rational; else only one point. So for it to contain three lattice points, we need m rational with denominator dividing the difference in x. But the points are bounded by the triangle; the line can intersect the triangle in a segment; the x-range of that segment is limited. For a line with slope not 0 or ∞ or -1, the line can intersect the triangle in a segment; the length of that segment (in terms of x) is at most something like (n+1) - something. But we need to check if it's possible to have three points with coordinates positive and sum ≤ n+1. For three points, the x-values would be x, x+d, x+2d for some integer d>0. Their y-values are y, y+md, y+2md. The sums: x+y, x+d+y+md, x+2d+y+2md. For these to be ≤ n+1, we need the largest sum ≤ n+1. So x+y+2d(1+m) ≤ n+1. Since x+y ≥ 2, and d≥1, this gives 2+2(1+m) ≤ n+1 => 2(2+m) ≤ n+1 => n ≥ 2(2+m)-1 = 2m+3. So for a given m, we need n sufficiently large. But m can be rational, and we can choose d appropriately. For example, take m=2. Then we need n ≥ 7? For n=7, triangle: a+b ≤8. Points: (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) sums: 3,6,9? 3+6=9>8, so (3,6) not in set. But could we have points like (1,1),(2,3),(3,5)? slope 2? (2-1)/(2-1)=1, not 2. So need points with slope 2: (x,y),(x+1,y+2),(x+2,y+4). Their sums: x+y, x+1+y+2 = x+y+3, x+2+y+4 = x+y+6. For these to be ≤ n+1, we need x+y+6 ≤ n+1 => n ≥ x+y+5. Since x+y≥2, n≥7. For n=7, x+y+6 ≤8 => x+y ≤2. So x=y=1. Then points: (1,1),(2,3),(3,5). Check if (2,3) sum=5 ≤8 yes; (3,5) sum=8 ≤8 yes. So for n=7, line through (1,1),(2,3),(3,5) has slope 2, and contains three points in the triangle (since a+b ≤8). But is this line sunny? Slope 2 is not horizontal, vertical, or -1, so yes it is sunny. So a sunny line can contain 3 points for n=7. So the claim that sunny lines contain at most 2 points is false for larger n. So we need to consider general possibilities.

Thus, we need to determine all k for which there exists a set of n lines covering all points in the triangle T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2: a+b ≤ n+1}. The lines can be arbitrary; they may not be rational slopes. But we can choose them to be rational slopes to contain many lattice points.

The problem is reminiscent of covering the triangular lattice points with lines, similar to problems about "blocking sets" or "covering points with lines". The condition that exactly k lines are sunny adds a twist.

We need to determine all possible k for a given n≥3. Since the problem statement: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So for each n, we need to characterize the set of k (depending on n) for which such a configuration exists. Probably the answer is: k can be any integer between 0 and n inclusive, except possibly some values depending on n? Or maybe only certain k are possible because of parity or something.

We need to analyze constraints.

Let's denote the set of points P = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2: a+b ≤ n+1}. The lines are L_1,...,L_n. We want each point in P to be on at least one line.

We have a constraint on the number of sunny lines. A line that is not sunny is either horizontal (parallel to x-axis), vertical (parallel to y-axis), or parallel to x+y=0 (i.e., slope -1). So these are lines that are "bad" directions.

We can think of the lines as possibly covering many points. For each line, we can count the number of points it covers from P. The sum over lines of these counts is at least |P| = n(n+1)/2 (since each point is covered by at least one line). So the average coverage per line is (n+1)/2.

Now, we need to see if there is any limitation on the number of sunny lines based on coverage. For a sunny line, its slope is not 0, ∞, or -1. So it can have any other slope. But we might be able to make a sunny line cover many points if we choose its slope rationally and align it with many lattice points. However, we have only n lines total, and we need to cover all points. Could we have all n lines sunny? For n=3 we did. For n=4 we need to see if it's possible. Let's try to construct for n=4 with all 4 sunny lines. Maybe we can use a pattern: For n=3 we used lines: y=x (slope 1), x+2y=5 (slope -1/2), 2x+y=5 (slope -2). These lines each contain one hypotenuse point. For n=4, we need 4 lines, each containing one of the points (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). We also need to cover other points. Could we use lines that are "reflections" of each other? For instance, take lines through (1,4) and (2,2): slope (2-4)/(2-1)=-2, line: 2x+y=6? Actually (1,4): 2+4=6; (2,2):4+2=6; yes. L3: contains H4=(4,1). L4: contains H1=(1,4). Now, we also need to cover points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3). We can assign them to L1,L2,L3 respectively (since L4 already has H1, but could also cover (1,1)? So let's decide: L1 contains (2,3) and (1,1). L2 contains (3,2) and (1,2). L3 contains (4,1) and (1,3). Now, we need to cover the remaining interior points: (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Also, note that (2,2) is interior; (2,1) interior; (3,1) interior. Also, (2,2) and (2,1) have x=2; they cannot be on L1 because L1 already has (2,3) and cannot have another x=2. So they must be on L2,L3,L4. Similarly, (3,1) has x=3; L2 already has (3,2); cannot have another x=3; so it must be on L1,L3,L4. (2,1) and (2,2) have x=2; L1 cannot, so L2,L3,L4. L2 contains (3,2); it could also contain (2,2) or (2,1) if the line passes through those. But L2 already contains (3,2) and (1,2). The line through (1,2) and (3,2) is horizontal? Actually (1,2) and (3,2) have same y, so line is y=2, which is horizontal; but that line is not sunny. However, we assigned L2 to contain (1,2) and (3,2); that line is y=2, which is horizontal, so it's not sunny. But we need all lines to be sunny. So this assignment fails because L2 would be horizontal. So we must ensure that lines are not horizontal/vertical/slope -1. So when we assign two points to a line, the line must be sunny. So we cannot have L2 containing (1,2) and (3,2) because that's horizontal. So we need to assign points to lines such that each line is sunny. That imposes restrictions: The two points assigned to a line must not be horizontal (i.e., same y) unless the line is horizontal, which is not sunny; similarly not vertical; and not slope -1. So we need to choose points that are not collinear in those directions.

So we need to assign each of the three left column points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) to one of the lines L1,L2,L3, but also ensure that the line through the hypotenuse point and that left column point is sunny. That means the slope must not be 0, ∞, or -1. So for each pair (H_i, (1,j)), we need the slope to be not 0, ∞, or -1. Let's compute slopes for each possible pairing:

- H2=(2,3) with (1,1): slope = (1-3)/(1-2) = (-2)/(-1)=2, which is sunny (since slope 2 not 0,∞,-1). So OK.
- H2 with (1,2): slope = (2-3)/(1-2) = (-1)/(-1)=1, sunny (slope 1, not -1,0,∞). OK.
- H2 with (1,3): slope = (3-3)/(1-2)=0/(-1)=0, horizontal, not sunny. So H2 cannot pair with (1,3) if we want sunny line.
- H3=(3,2) with (1,1): slope = (1-2)/(1-3) = (-1)/(-2)=1/2, sunny.
- H3 with (1,2): slope = (2-2)/(1-3)=0/(-2)=0, horizontal, not sunny.
- H3 with (1,3): slope = (3-2)/(1-3)=1/(-2)=-1/2, sunny (slope -1/2).
- H4=(4,1) with (1,1): slope = (1-1)/(1-4)=0/(-3)=0, horizontal, not sunny.
- H4 with (1,2): slope = (2-1)/(1-4)=1/(-3)=-1/3, sunny.
- H4 with (1,3): slope = (3-1)/(1-4)=2/(-3)=-2/3, sunny.

So the allowed pairings: For H2, allowed left points: (1,1) and (1,2); not (1,3). For H3, allowed: (1,1) and (1,3); not (1,2). For H4, allowed: (1,2) and (1,3); not (1,1). So we have three left points and three hypotenuse points, and we need to assign each left point to a distinct hypotenuse point such that the pairing is allowed. This is like a bipartite matching. The allowed assignments: H2 can take (1,1) or (1,2); H3 can take (1,1) or (1,3); H4 can take (1,2) or (1,3). We need to assign each of the three left points to one of the hypotenuse points. Is there a perfect matching? Let's list possibilities: If we assign (1,1) to H2, then (1,2) could go to H4, (1,3) to H3? Check H4 with (1,2) is allowed, H3 with (1,3) is allowed. So that works: L1: H2-(1,1); L2: H3-(1,3); L3: H4-(1,2). But then we also have H1=(1,4) assigned to L4; L4 contains (1,4) and maybe also some other points? But L4 must be sunny; it could contain other points, but we need to cover the remaining interior points (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Let's see if we can cover them with these lines.

So we have lines:
L1: through H2=(2,3) and (1,1). Equation: slope =2, line: y-3 = 2(x-2) => y=2x-1? Check (1,1):1=2-1=1 yes. So L1: y=2x-1. This line contains (1,1) and (2,3). Also (3,5) not needed; (0,-1) no. So covers points: (1,1) and (2,3). Also could cover (3,5) not in set; (4,7) no.
L2: through H3=(3,2) and (1,3). Slope = (1-2)/(1-3) = (-1)/(-2)=1/2? Actually we need to compute line through (3,2) and (1,3): slope = (3-2)/(1-3)=1/(-2)=-1/2. So slope -1/2. Equation: y-2 = -1/2(x-3) => y = -1/2 x + 3/2 + 2 = -1/2 x + 7/2? Let's compute: y = -1/2 x + 7/2. Check (1,3): -1/2+7/2=6/2=3 yes. So L2: y = -1/2 x + 7/2. This line contains (1,3) and (3,2). Also (5,1) not in set? (5,1): -5/2+7/2=2/2=1 yes, but (5,1) sum=6>5, not required. So covers (1,3) and (3,2).
L3: through H4=(4,1) and (1,2). Slope = (2-1)/(1-4)=1/(-3)=-1/3. Equation: y-1 = -1/3(x-4) => y = -1/3 x + 4/3 + 1 = -1/3 x + 7/3. Check (1,2): -1/3+7/3=6/3=2 yes. So L3: y = -1/3 x + 7/3. Contains (4,1) and (1,2).
L4: through H1=(1,4). Need to be sunny, so it can contain other points, but must be non-horizontal/vertical/slope -1. It could contain some of the remaining interior points. We need to cover (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Also note that (2,1) and (2,2) have x=2; (3,1) has x=3. Can we assign them to L4? L4 contains (1,4). We need to choose a line through (1,4) that is sunny and contains as many of these points as possible. But we only have L4 as the fourth line; it must cover all remaining points. So we need to find a line L4 (sunny) that contains (1,4) and also contains (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). However, can a single line contain three points with different x? Possibly, if they are collinear. Are (1,4),(2,1),(3,? ) collinear? Slope between (1,4) and (2,1) is (1-4)/(2-1)=-3, so line: y-4 = -3(x-1) => y = -3x+7. For x=3, y = -9+7=-2, not 1. So (3,1) not on it. Could we have line through (1,4) and (2,2): slope (2-4)/(2-1)=-2, line: y=-2x+6; for x=3, y=-6+6=0, not 1. So not. So we need to cover (2,1),(2,2),(3,1) with lines that already exist? But we already used L1,L2,L3; they only cover two points each, and none of the remaining points are on those lines (check: L1: y=2x-1, for x=2 gives y=3, so (2,3) covered; x=3 gives y=5 not; x=1 gives y=1; so (2,1) not; (2,2) not; (3,1) not. Let's systematically analyze the matching constraints for n=4.

We have points P. We need to cover them with 4 lines L1,...,L4, all sunny. For each line, it contains a set of points from P. Since lines are distinct and sunny, they have slopes not 0,∞,-1.

We need to cover all 10 points. There are 4 lines, each can contain many points. But we also have the condition that the points on the hypotenuse (a+b=5) are H1=(1,4), H2=(2,3), H3=(3,2), H4=(4,1). Each sunny line can contain at most one of these points. Since there are 4 lines and 4 hypotenuse points, each line must contain exactly one hypotenuse point. So we have a bijection between lines and hypotenuse points.

Now, the left column points (a=1) are L1'=(1,1), L2'=(1,2), L3'=(1,3), L4'=(1,4). Note that (1,4) is a hypotenuse point. Each of those lines already contains a hypotenuse point; they can also contain at most one point from column a=1 (since if they contain two points with x=1, they would be vertical, not sunny). So we need to assign each of (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) to a distinct line among the three lines containing H2,H3,H4. So that's a matching.

Similarly, the bottom row points (b=1) are (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). (4,1) is a hypotenuse point. So the points (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) must be covered by lines that are not vertical? We can try to find a valid configuration by considering possible slopes.

Let's denote the three lines (not containing H1) as L2 (contains H2), L3 (contains H3), L4 (contains H4). - For H2 with (1,1): line y=2x-1. (2,1): y=3 no; (2,2): y=3 no; (3,1): y=5 no.
- For H2 with (1,2): line y=x+1. (2,1): y=3 no; (2,2): y=3 no; (3,1): y=4 no.
- For H3 with (1,1): line y=0.5x+0.5. (2,1): y=1.5 no; (2,2): y=1.5 no; (3,1): y=2 no.
- For H3 with (1,3): line y=-0.5x+3.5. (2,1): y=-1+3.5=2.5 no; (2,2):2.5 no; (3,1): -1.5+3.5=2, so (3,2) covered, not (3,1). So (3,1) not.
- For H4 with (1,2): line y=-1/3 x+7/3. (2,1): -2/3+7/3=5/3≈1.667 no; (2,2):5/3 no; (3,1): -1+7/3=4/3≈1.333 no.
- For H4 with (1,3): line y=-2/3 x+11/3. (2,1): -4/3+11/3=7/3≈2.333 no; (2,2):2.333 no; (3,1): -2+11/3=5/3≈1.667 no.

So none of these lines contain any of the interior points. So indeed, if we assign each left point to a distinct line, the only points covered by those lines are the hypotenuse points and the left points, no interior points. Then the interior points must be covered by L1. But L1 cannot cover all three because they are not collinear with (1,4). So n=4 with all 4 sunny is impossible.

But could we have a configuration where L1 (through H1) also contains one of the left points? That would be vertical, not sunny. So no.

Thus, k=4 is impossible for n=4.

What about k=3 for n=4? That means exactly 3 sunny lines, 1 non-sunny line. We can use a non-sunny line to cover many points, e.g., a horizontal line y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). That's 4 points. Then we need 3 sunny lines to cover the rest: points with b≥2, and also maybe (2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) is covered by y=1? Actually (4,1) is covered by y=1; (1,1) also; (2,1); (3,1). So remaining points: (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's 6 points. Need to cover them with 3 sunny lines. Is that possible? Likely yes. For n=4, we can try to construct.

Alternatively, use a vertical line x=1 covering (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). That's 4 points. Then remaining: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1) — 6 points. Need 3 sunny lines. Possibly.

Use line x+y=5 (hypotenuse) covering (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 4 points. Then remaining: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1) — 6 points. Need 3 sunny lines. Possibly.

We need to check if there exists a configuration with exactly k sunny lines for any k from 0 to n. Probably the answer is: all k from 0 to n-2? Or something like that. Let's try to determine for general n.

We have triangle T_n. We need to cover all points. Let's denote the set of points on the hypotenuse: H = {(a, n+1-a): a=1,...,n}. There are n points. Each sunny line can contain at most one of these points. So if we have s sunny lines, they can cover at most s points from H. The remaining n-s points from H must be covered by non-sunny lines. A non-sunny line can be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. The only non-sunny line that can cover more than one point from H is the hypotenuse line itself (x+y=n+1). A horizontal line y=c can cover at most one point from H (since b varies, only one point with that y). Similarly vertical. So if we want to cover all n hypotenuse points, we need at least n-s non-sunny lines that are the hypotenuse line? But we have only n total lines. So we need to cover the hypotenuse points with lines. There are two extremes: Either use the line x+y=n+1, which is non-sunny, and then it covers all n hypotenuse points with one line. Then we have n-1 lines left to cover the rest of the points (with sum ≤ n). That's a triangle of size n-1. Or we can cover the hypotenuse points using n lines (each covering one point), which could be any lines (sunny or not). But if we use n lines, we have no lines left for other points, so each line must also cover some other points. In that case, all n lines are used for the hypotenuse points, and each must be sunny? Not necessarily; they could be non-sunny, but then the number of sunny lines would be less than n. So we need to consider both strategies.

The problem asks: For given n, determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying the covering condition and exactly k of them are sunny. So we need to characterize possible k.

Observation: The covering condition is quite strong. It might be that the only possible configurations are those where the lines are either all horizontal (y=1,...,n) giving k=0, or all vertical (x=1,...,n) giving k=0, or all slope -1 (x+y=2,...,n+1) giving k=0, or some mix. But we already found for n=3 a configuration with all 3 sunny lines. So k=3 works for n=3. For n=4, we suspect k=4 is impossible, but maybe k=3 works, and k=2,1,0 work. Let's test n=4 with k=3: we need 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. Can we construct? Let's try to use the non-sunny line as the hypotenuse line x+y=5. That covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Then we have 3 sunny lines to cover the remaining points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 6 points. Need 3 sunny lines covering all 6 points. Each sunny line can contain at most one of the points on the "left boundary" (a=1) and bottom boundary (b=1)? Actually, we need to cover these points. Since we have 3 lines, they need to cover 6 points. Each line could cover 2 points on average. That's plausible. We need to find 3 sunny lines such that their union contains these 6 points. Also, the lines must be distinct and sunny. Let's attempt.

Points: P = {(1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1)}. We need 3 sunny lines covering all. Let's try to use lines that are like: y=x? That covers (1,1),(2,2) — (2,2) is in set; (1,1) is in set. That's 4 lines, with 3 sunny (L1,L2,L3) and 1 non-sunny (L4). So k=3 works for n=4. Good.

Now, we need to prove that k=2 is impossible. Let's attempt a general proof.

We have n distinct lines L_1,...,L_n. Exactly k of them are sunny. We need to cover all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1.

We already know that k=0 works (all horizontal lines). So we need to show that if k=2, then it's impossible. Similarly, if k≥4? We can attempt to prove an upper bound on the number of sunny lines based on the covering requirement. For a given n, the points on the hypotenuse are n points, each must be covered by a line. A sunny line can cover at most one hypotenuse point (since if a sunny line covered two hypotenuse points, it would be the hypotenuse line, which is not sunny). So the number of sunny lines s = k must be at least the number of hypotenuse points that are covered by sunny lines. But since we have n lines total, the remaining n-s lines are non-sunny, and they can cover the remaining hypotenuse points. However, we need to cover all points, not just hypotenuse. So perhaps we can derive that s cannot be 2 because the non-sunny lines cannot cover all points? We need a rigorous argument.

One approach: Consider the set of points with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n such points. Each line that is not parallel to x+y=0 can intersect the hypotenuse at at most one point. So any line that is sunny cannot contain two hypotenuse points; a line that is parallel to x+y=0 (slope -1) can contain at most one hypotenuse point if it's not the hypotenuse itself? Actually, if a line is parallel to x+y=0, it's of form x+y = d. For d = n+1, it contains all hypotenuse points; for d ≠ n+1, it contains no hypotenuse points. So the only non-sunny line that can contain multiple hypotenuse points is the hypotenuse itself (x+y=n+1). So if we have s sunny lines, they cover at most s hypotenuse points. The remaining n-s hypotenuse points must be covered by non-sunny lines. But the non-sunny lines can be of three types: horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. A horizontal line y=c can contain at most one hypotenuse point (since only one point on hypotenuse has that b). Similarly vertical. A slope -1 line can contain either all n hypotenuse points if it's the hypotenuse, or none otherwise. So to cover the remaining n-s hypotenuse points, we need at least n-s non-sunny lines that are not slope -1? Or we can use the hypotenuse line to cover all n points, in which case we need only 1 non-sunny line to cover all hypotenuse points. So the number of non-sunny lines can be as low as 1 if we use the hypotenuse line. That's allowed. So the number of sunny lines can be up to n (if we use only sunny lines). But we already have a construction with 3 sunny lines for any n, so the maximum s is at least 3. So s can be 0,1,3. Could s be 2? Let's try to see if there is any parity or other constraint that prevents s=2.

We can consider the following: For each line, count the number of points from P that lie on it. The sum over all lines of these counts is at least |P| = n(n+1)/2. If we have three collinear points with slope not 0,∞,-1, then the line can be described by equation y = mx + c with m rational. Thus, we have a construction for k=3 for all n≥3.

Now, we need to show that k=2 is impossible. Let's attempt a general proof for n≥3.

Assume we have n lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1. Exactly k of these lines are sunny. We want to show that if k=2, then it's impossible.

We can use the following approach: Consider the points on the hypotenuse H = {(1,n), (2,n-1), ..., (n,1)}. There are n points. Each line that is sunny can contain at most one of these points. The non-sunny lines can contain multiple hypotenuse points, but only the line x+y=n+1 can contain all n points. If we use that line as a non-sunny line, then we can cover all hypotenuse points with one line. Otherwise, we need at least n non-sunny lines to cover the n hypotenuse points (since each non-sunny line can contain at most one hypotenuse point if it's not the hypotenuse itself). So the number of non-sunny lines must be at least n if we don't use the hypotenuse line. But we have n lines total, so if we don't use the hypotenuse line, then all lines are non-sunny (since s=2 sunny lines, so non-sunny = n-2). That would give n-2 lines to cover n hypotenuse points, but each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 hypotenuse point (if it's horizontal or vertical) or possibly many if it's the hypotenuse. But if we don't use the hypotenuse line, then each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 hypotenuse point. So we would need at least n non-sunny lines to cover n points, but we have only n-2 non-sunny lines. Therefore, it's impossible to cover all hypotenuse points if we don't use the hypotenuse line. Thus, we must use the hypotenuse line x+y=n+1 as one of the non-sunny lines. So let L_h: x+y=n+1 be one of the lines. Since we have exactly 2 sunny lines, the remaining n-1 lines are non-sunny. One of them is the hypotenuse. The other n-2 non-sunny lines are either horizontal or vertical (since they are not sunny, they are parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0; but the only slope -1 line we use is the hypotenuse, so others are horizontal or vertical). So we have n-2 horizontal or vertical lines, plus the hypotenuse line. Now, these n-2 lines can cover some of the boundary points.

Consider the set of points with a=1 (the left column). There are n points: (1,1), (1,2), ..., (1,n). The line x=1 is vertical; if we have x=1 as a non-sunny line, it covers all these points. If we don't have x=1, then each of these points must be covered by some other line. A sunny line can contain at most one point from this column (since it's not vertical). A horizontal line y=c can contain at most one point from this column (the point (1,c) if c is between 1 and n). So to cover all n points in the left column, we would need at least n lines that each cover at most one point, which is impossible if we have only 2 sunny lines and n-2 other lines. But note: The hypotenuse line x+y=n+1 contains (1,n) only; it covers one point. So we could cover (1,n) with the hypotenuse. Then we need to cover the remaining n-1 points in the left column. Each of these must be covered by a line that is either sunny, horizontal, or vertical. But a sunny line can cover at most one of them, and a horizontal line can cover at most one of them (since it's y=c, it covers (1,c) only). A vertical line x=1 would cover all of them, but that's a single line. So if we have x=1 as a non-sunny line, it covers all left column points. So we need to include x=1 as one of the non-sunny lines. Similarly, consider the bottom row b=1: points (1,1), (2,1), ..., (n,1). The line y=1 is horizontal and covers all these points. So we need to include y=1 as a non-sunny line to cover the bottom row. The hypotenuse covers (n,1). So we need to cover the other points. So to cover all points, we likely need to have both x=1 and y=1 as non-sunny lines. But we have n-2 non-sunny lines besides the hypotenuse; we can include x=1 and y=1 as two of them. Then we have n-4 other non-sunny lines that can be chosen arbitrarily (horizontal or vertical). Now, after using x=1, y=1, and the hypotenuse, what points are left? Points with a≥2, b≥2, and a+b ≤ n (since points with a+b=n+1 are covered by hypotenuse, points with a=1 or b=1 are covered by x=1 and y=1). So the uncovered points are exactly the triangle of points with a≥2,b≥2,a+b≤n. Now, we have only 2 sunny lines left to cover these points. So we need to show that 2 sunny lines cannot cover all points of the triangle T' = {(a,b) : a≥2, b≥2, a+b≤n}. Let's denote m = n-2? The points in T' have coordinates between 2 and n-2. The number of such points is (n-3)(n-2)/2 for n≥4? For n=5, that's 2*3/2=3; for n=6, that's 3*4/2=6; for n=7, 4*5/2=10; etc. So we need to show that for any n≥5, 2 sunny lines cannot cover all points of T'. For n=5, we already saw it's impossible because the 3 points are not collinear on a sunny line; any line through two of them is not sunny. For n=6, we have 6 points; we argued each sunny line contains at most 2 points from T', so 2 lines cover at most 4 points, leaving 2 uncovered. For n=7, we have 10 points; we need to see the maximum points a sunny line can contain from T'. Is it possible that a sunny line contains 3 points from T'? For n=7, T' includes points with a,b≥2, a+b≤7. Let's list: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(2,5); (3,2),(3,3),(3,4); (4,2),(4,3); (5,2). That's 4+3+2+1=10 points. Now, we need to show that any sunny line can contain at most 2 points from this triangle? But we saw for n=8, y=x contains 3 points (2,2),(3,3),(4,4). So that's false. So we need to consider the specific non-sunny lines we choose. In our construction, we used x=1 and y=1 and the hypotenuse to cover the boundary, leaving T'. But we also have the option to use additional horizontal lines y=2,...,y=n-5 to reduce T' to a smaller set. Thus, if we use the general construction with non-sunny lines y=1..n-5, x=1, and hypotenuse, the uncovered set has 6 points. To cover them with 2 sunny lines, we would need each sunny line to cover at least 3 points, but we can show that any sunny line can contain at most 2 points from this specific 6-point set. Why? Because the set is a small triangle of 6 points; any line through two of them is either horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. Check: Points are (2,4),(3,4),(4,4),(2,5),(3,5),(2,6). Lines:

- Horizontal: y=4 contains (2,4),(3,4),(4,4) — 3 points, but slope 0, not sunny.
- Vertical: x=2 contains (2,4),(2,5),(2,6) — 3 points, slope infinite, not sunny.
- Slope -1: (2,4) and (3,3) not; (2,4) and (4,2) not; (2,5) and (3,4) slope -1? (4-5)/(3-2) = -1, so (2,5) and (3,4) are on line x+y=7? (2,5) sum=7, (3,4) sum=7, so that's a line of slope -1, not sunny.
- Slope -2: (2,4) and (3,2) no; (2,5) and (3,1) no; (2,6) and (3,0) no.
- Slope -0.5: (2,4) and (4,3) no; (2,5) and (4,2) slope -1.5; (3,5) and (4,3) slope -2; etc.

Thus, no line through two points from this set has slope other than 0,∞,-1. So any sunny line (slope not 0,∞,-1) can contain at most one point from this set. Actually, could a sunny line contain two points from this set? If it contains two points, then the slope between them must be the slope of the line; if that slope is not 0,∞,-1, then it's sunny. But we argued that any two points from this set have slopes either 0 (if same y), ∞ (if same x), or -1 (if x+y constant). Indeed, let's verify: Take two points (a,b) and (c,d) from the set. The possible values: The x-coordinates are 2,3,4; y-coordinates are 4,5,6. If a=c, then slope infinite; if b=d, then slope 0; if a+b = c+d, then slope -1. For any pair, is it possible that a≠c, b≠d, and a+b≠c+d? For example, (2,4) and (3,5): a=2,c=3,b=4,d=5; a≠c, b≠d, a+b=6, c+d=8, not equal; slope = (5-4)/(3-2)=1, not 0,∞,-1. So slope 1 is possible! Wait, (2,4) and (3,5): slope = (5-4)/(3-2)=1, which is sunny. So a sunny line can contain these two points? Check: line through (2,4) and (3,5) has slope 1, equation y=x+2. This line is sunny. Does it contain any other points from the set? For x=4, y=6, but (4,6) is in set? (4,6) has sum=10>8, so not in set. So it contains exactly two points from the set. So a sunny line can contain 2 points from this 6-point set. So with 2 sunny lines, we could potentially cover 4 points (each covering 2 points), leaving 2 uncovered. Could we choose the two sunny lines to cover all 6 points? Possibly if one sunny line covers 2 points and the other covers the remaining 4 points? But the other line would need to cover 4 points; can a sunny line cover 4 points from this set? Check if any line can contain 3 points: we already have horizontal, vertical, slope -1 lines containing 3 points, but those are not sunny. What about a line with slope 2? Through (2,4) and (3,6) but (3,6) not in set; through (2,5) and (3,7) no; through (2,4) and (4,6) slope 1, line y=x+2, which contains (2,4),(3,5),(4,6) but (4,6) not in set. So only 2 points. Could a line with slope -0.5 contain two points? (2,4) and (4,2) no; (2,5) and (4,1) no; (3,5) and (4,3) no. So no line contains 3 points. So each sunny line can contain at most 2 points. So with 2 sunny lines, max coverage is 4 points. Thus, 2 sunny lines cannot cover all 6 points. So s=2 impossible.

Thus, for n≥5, if we try to cover all points with 2 sunny lines, we must include x=1, y=1, and the hypotenuse as non-sunny lines (since otherwise we cannot cover the boundary). After that, the uncovered set is T' with 6 points (the small triangle). And 2 sunny lines cannot cover those 6 points. Therefore, s=2 is impossible for n≥5.

For n=3, we have a construction with 3 sunny lines, and s=2 is impossible because we can check: n=3, points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Can we have 2 sunny lines covering all? The points are 6; each sunny line can contain at most 2 points? Actually, y=x covers (1,1),(2,2) — 2 points; y=x+1 covers (1,2),(2,3) but (2,3) not in set; so (1,2) only; y=-2x+? Could cover (1,3),(2,1) slope -2; that's 2 points. So with 2 lines, max 4 points; so impossible. So s=2 fails for n=3.

Thus, k=2 is impossible for all n≥3.

Thus, k=4 is impossible for n=4. For larger n, similar reasoning: the number of hypotenuse points is n. If we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k hypotenuse points. The remaining n-k hypotenuse points must be covered by non-sunny lines. If we don't use the hypotenuse line, we need at least n-k non-sunny lines to cover those points, each covering at most one (since they are not the hypotenuse line). So the total number of non-sunny lines must be at least n-k. But we have n-k lines non-sunny? Actually, total lines = n, sunny = k, non-sunny = n-k. So if we don't use the hypotenuse line, we need n-k non-sunny lines to cover n-k hypotenuse points, but each non-sunny line can cover at most one hypotenuse point, so we need at least n-k non-sunny lines, which is exactly the number we have. So that is possible if we have exactly n-k non-sunny lines and we use them to cover the remaining hypotenuse points, each covering one. That would require that all non-sunny lines are either horizontal or vertical and each contains exactly one hypotenuse point. That is possible if we choose the non-sunny lines to be, for example, y = 1 (covers (1,1) only? Actually, (1,1) is not a hypotenuse point because hypotenuse points have a+b=n+1, so (1,1) sum=2, not. So a horizontal line y=c covers only the hypotenuse point (n+1-c, c) if that point exists. So if we use horizontal lines y=1,...,n-1, each covers one hypotenuse point (except possibly the top). Similarly, vertical lines x=c cover one hypotenuse point (c, n+1-c). So it's possible to cover all hypotenuse points with n-1 non-sunny lines (if we use, say, y=1,...,n-1, that covers (n,1), (n-1,2), ..., (1,n) — that's all n points). So we could have non-sunny lines that are horizontal lines covering all hypotenuse points. But then we have no sunny lines? Wait, if we have k sunny lines, we have n-k non-sunny lines. If we use them to cover the hypotenuse points, we need at least n non-sunny lines to cover all n hypotenuse points (if each covers one). But we have only n-k non-sunny lines. So to cover n hypotenuse points, we need n non-sunny lines if we don't use the hypotenuse line. So we must have k=0 if we don't use the hypotenuse line. But if k>0, we could use the hypotenuse line to cover all n points with one non-sunny line, and then the remaining non-sunny lines can be used for other points. So for k≥1, we must include the hypotenuse line as a non-sunny line. So the only way to have k sunny lines is to have the hypotenuse line as one of the non-sunny lines. Then the remaining n-1 lines are non-sunny, and we have k sunny lines. So the total number of non-sunny lines is n-k, but we already used 1 for the hypotenuse, so we have n-k-1 other non-sunny lines that must be either horizontal or vertical. Those can be used to cover some of the boundary points. Then the uncovered points are those not covered by the non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover at most one column (x=1) and at most one row (y=1) if we include them. But we have n-k-1 other lines; they could be used to cover additional boundary points. For k≥4, we need to see if we can cover all points. For k=4, we have 4 sunny lines, and non-sunny = n-4. One of them is the hypotenuse, so we have n-5 other non-sunny lines. To cover the left column (a=1) with n points, we could use x=1 as a non-sunny line, covering all left column points. Similarly, we could use y=1 as a non-sunny line to cover all bottom row points. That would use 2 lines, but we have only n-5 lines left, which for n≥5 is at most? For n=5, n-5=0, so we can't use x=1 and y=1 because we have only 0 lines besides hypotenuse. So we would need to cover the left column and bottom row with the sunny lines. But sunny lines can cover at most one point from each column. So to cover all n left column points, we would need at least n sunny lines, but we have only 4 sunny lines. Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: for all positive integers a and b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines; and exactly k of the n lines are sunny."

Interpretation: We have n distinct lines. The condition: For all positive integers a,b with a+b ≤ n+1, the point (a,b) lies on at least one of the lines. So we need to cover all integer lattice points in the first quadrant (a,b positive integers) with a+b ≤ n+1. That's a triangular set of points: (1,1), (1,2), ..., (1,n), (2,1), ..., (n,1) up to (n,1) and also points like (2,2) etc. Actually a+b ≤ n+1 means a from 1 to n, b from 1 to n+1-a. So total number of points = sum_{a=1}^{n} (n+1-a) = n(n+1)/2. For n≥3, it's at least 6.

We need to select n distinct lines such that every such point lies on at least one line. Additionally, exactly k of these n lines are "sunny", meaning not parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or line x+y=0. So lines that are not parallel to these three directions are sunny; lines that are parallel to one of these three are not sunny.

We need to determine for which k (nonnegative integer) such a configuration exists.

We must consider lines in the plane; they can be any lines (not necessarily through origin). The condition is about covering points.

We need to find all possible k. Probably k can be any integer from something to something, but with constraints due to the number of lines and covering requirement.

Let's denote L = set of n lines. For each line, it is either sunny or not sunny. The number of sunny lines is k.

We need to determine existence for given n and k.

We have to consider the geometry: The points (a,b) are integer points in the first quadrant, bounded by the line a+b = n+1. So the convex hull of these points is a right triangle with vertices (1,1), (n,1), (1,n). Actually points: (1,1) to (n,1) along bottom, (1,1) to (1,n) along left, and the hypotenuse from (n,1) to (1,n) with equation a+b = n+1. So the points are exactly the lattice points inside that triangle (including boundaries). But we need to cover each point with at least one line.

Lines: Each line can be described by an equation: Ax+By+C=0, with (A,B) not both zero. The condition "not parallel to x-axis" means A=0? Actually x-axis is y=0, so lines parallel to x-axis have equation y = constant (i.e., B=0 in Ax+By+C=0? Wait: The line y = c is 0*x + 1*y - c = 0, so B=1, A=0. So parallel to x-axis: A=0. Similarly, parallel to y-axis: B=0. Parallel to line x+y=0: lines of the form x+y = d, so A=1, B=1 (or multiples). So sunny lines are those not having (A,B) proportional to (0,1), (1,0), or (1,1). In other words, they are lines whose direction is not horizontal, vertical, or slope -1.

Now, we need to cover a set of points. Lines can cover many points; but we have exactly n lines. The points count is n(n+1)/2. So average coverage per line is (n+1)/2. Since lines are straight, they can cover at most something. But we need existence for certain k.

We need to determine constraints on k based on n.

Observation: The points (a,b) with a+b = n+1 (the hypotenuse) are n points: (1,n), (2,n-1), ..., (n,1). They lie on the line x+y = n+1. That line is not sunny because it is parallel to x+y=0 (since direction slope -1). So if we use that line, it is not sunny. But we might cover those points with other lines.

Also, the points along the axes? But a and b are positive integers, so no points on axes. So the points are all in the interior and boundary of the triangle.

We need to find the maximum possible number of non-sunny lines we can have while still covering all points. And also the minimum possible number of sunny lines? Actually k is the number of sunny lines; we want all k such that there exists a configuration with exactly k sunny lines.

We can choose lines arbitrarily; we can also choose lines that are not sunny (i.e., horizontal, vertical, or slope -1). The condition that exactly k lines are sunny means we have n-k lines that are not sunny.

We need to see if there is any restriction on k based on n.

Let's try to construct configurations.

First, note that we can always cover all points with n lines that are all horizontal? But horizontal lines are not sunny. However, we need to cover all points: For each point (a,b), we need some line containing it. If we use only horizontal lines (y = constant), we can cover each row of points: all points with same b. Since b ranges from 1 to n, we have n rows, each row has points with a from 1 to n+1-b. So we can take horizontal lines y=1, y=2, ..., y=n. That's n lines. All are horizontal, so none are sunny (since parallel to x-axis). So k=0 is achievable. But need distinct lines: yes, y=1,...,y=n are distinct. So k=0 works.

Similarly, we can use vertical lines x=1,...,x=n. That covers each column: all points with same a. That's also n lines, all vertical, so k=0 works. Also, we can use lines parallel to x+y=0: lines x+y = d. For d from 2 to n+1? Actually points: For each d from 2 to n+1, line x+y = d contains points (a,b) with a+b=d, a,b≥1. So we can take lines x+y = 2,3,...,n+1. That's n lines. But note: The line x+y = 2 contains only (1,1). That's fine. So all lines are slope -1, so none are sunny. So k=0 works.

Thus k=0 is possible for all n≥3.

Now, what about k>0? We need to have some sunny lines. But we must cover all points. Since we have exactly n lines, we can possibly replace some of the non-sunny lines with sunny lines, but we need to cover all points. The question: For a given n, what are all possible k? Possibly all k from 0 to n? But is there any restriction? For example, can we have all n lines sunny? Possibly yes, but we need to cover all points. Let's think.

So indeed, a line with slope not 0, ∞, -1 can contain at most 2 points from the set? Not necessarily; consider a line through (1,1) and (3,2): slope 1/2, points: (1,1) sum=2; (3,2) sum=5; (5,3) sum=8 >5; so only 2. Another: line through (1,1) and (4,3): slope 2/3, points: (1,1) sum=2; (4,3) sum=7>5; so only 1. So it seems that any line that is not horizontal/vertical/slope -1 can contain at most 2 points from the triangle (since the points are in a convex set with a boundary that is not parallel to the line; the line can intersect the triangle in a segment, but the lattice points on that segment are limited because the triangle is small). But is it possible to have a line that contains 3 points? For example, consider n=5, triangle: a+b ≤6. Points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,1),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1). Could there be a line with slope 1/2 that goes through (1,1),(2,2)? That's slope 1, not 1/2. To have slope 1/2, points like (1,1) and (3,2): slope (2-1)/(3-1)=1/2; (1,1) sum=2; (3,2) sum=5; (5,3) sum=8 >6? Actually 5+3=8>6, so only two. Could have (2,1) and (4,2): slope 1/2, (2,1) sum=3; (4,2) sum=6; (6,3) sum=9>6; so two. Could have (1,2) and (3,3): slope 1/2? (3-2)/(3-1)=1/2; (1,2) sum=3; (3,3) sum=6; (5,4) sum=9>6; so two. So seems only two. Could have a line with slope -2 that goes through (1,4) and (2,2): slope (2-4)/(2-1)=-2; (1,4) sum=5; (2,2) sum=4; (3,0) not; so two. Possibly a line with slope 1/3 through (1,1) and (4,2): slope 1/3, points: (1,1) sum=2; (4,2) sum=6; (7,3) sum=10>6; so two. So it appears that for any line not horizontal/vertical/slope -1, it can contain at most 2 points from the set of points with a+b ≤ n+1. But is that always true? Consider a line with slope 2 that passes through (1,2) and (2,4): (2,4) sum=6>5 for n=4; for n=5, (1,2) sum=3; (2,4) sum=6; (3,6) sum=9>6; so only two. For n=6, triangle: a+b ≤7. Points: (1,1)...(6,1) etc. Could a line with slope 2 go through (1,2) and (3,4)? (1,2) sum=3; (3,4) sum=7; (5,6) sum=11>7; so two. Could it go through (2,1) and (4,3)? slope (3-1)/(4-2)=1; no. So it seems the maximum number of lattice points on a line inside a right triangle with legs along axes and hypotenuse of slope -1 is limited. In general, if a line has slope m, then the lattice points on it are of the form (x0 + t, y0 + mt) for integers t, if m is rational; else only one point. So for it to contain three lattice points, we need m rational with denominator dividing the difference in x. But the points are bounded by the triangle; the line can intersect the triangle in a segment; the x-range of that segment is limited. For a line with slope not 0 or ∞ or -1, the line can intersect the triangle in a segment; the length of that segment (in terms of x) is at most something like (n+1) - something. But we need to check if it's possible to have three points with coordinates positive and sum ≤ n+1. For three points, the x-values would be x, x+d, x+2d for some integer d>0. Their y-values are y, y+md, y+2md. The sums: x+y, x+d+y+md, x+2d+y+2md. For these to be ≤ n+1, we need the largest sum ≤ n+1. So x+y+2d(1+m) ≤ n+1. Since x+y ≥ 2, and d≥1, this gives 2+2(1+m) ≤ n+1 => 2(2+m) ≤ n+1 => n ≥ 2(2+m)-1 = 2m+3. So for a given m, we need n sufficiently large. But m can be rational, and we can choose d appropriately. For example, take m=2. Then we need n ≥ 7? For n=7, triangle: a+b ≤8. Points: (1,2),(2,4),(3,6) sums: 3,6,9? 3+6=9>8, so (3,6) not in set. But could we have points like (1,1),(2,3),(3,5)? slope 2? (2-1)/(2-1)=1, not 2. So need points with slope 2: (x,y),(x+1,y+2),(x+2,y+4). Their sums: x+y, x+1+y+2 = x+y+3, x+2+y+4 = x+y+6. For these to be ≤ n+1, we need x+y+6 ≤ n+1 => n ≥ x+y+5. Since x+y≥2, n≥7. For n=7, x+y+6 ≤8 => x+y ≤2. So x=y=1. Then points: (1,1),(2,3),(3,5). Check if (2,3) sum=5 ≤8 yes; (3,5) sum=8 ≤8 yes. So for n=7, line through (1,1),(2,3),(3,5) has slope 2, and contains three points in the triangle (since a+b ≤8). But is this line sunny? Slope 2 is not horizontal, vertical, or -1, so yes it is sunny. So a sunny line can contain 3 points for n=7. So the claim that sunny lines contain at most 2 points is false for larger n. So we need to consider general possibilities.

Thus, we need to determine all k for which there exists a set of n lines covering all points in the triangle T_n = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2: a+b ≤ n+1}. The lines can be arbitrary; they may not be rational slopes. But we can choose them to be rational slopes to contain many lattice points.

The problem is reminiscent of covering the triangular lattice points with lines, similar to problems about "blocking sets" or "covering points with lines". The condition that exactly k lines are sunny adds a twist.

We need to determine all possible k for a given n≥3. Since the problem statement: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both of the following: ... and exactly k of the n lines are sunny." So for each n, we need to characterize the set of k (depending on n) for which such a configuration exists. Probably the answer is: k can be any integer between 0 and n inclusive, except possibly some values depending on n? Or maybe only certain k are possible because of parity or something.

We need to analyze constraints.

Let's denote the set of points P = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2: a+b ≤ n+1}. The lines are L_1,...,L_n. We want each point in P to be on at least one line.

We have a constraint on the number of sunny lines. A line that is not sunny is either horizontal (parallel to x-axis), vertical (parallel to y-axis), or parallel to x+y=0 (i.e., slope -1). So these are lines that are "bad" directions.

We can think of the lines as possibly covering many points. For each line, we can count the number of points it covers from P. The sum over lines of these counts is at least |P| = n(n+1)/2 (since each point is covered by at least one line). So the average coverage per line is (n+1)/2.

Now, we need to see if there is any limitation on the number of sunny lines based on coverage. For a sunny line, its slope is not 0, ∞, or -1. So it can have any other slope. But we might be able to make a sunny line cover many points if we choose its slope rationally and align it with many lattice points. However, we have only n lines total, and we need to cover all points. Could we have all n lines sunny? For n=3 we did. For n=4 we need to see if it's possible. Let's try to construct for n=4 with all 4 sunny lines. Maybe we can use a pattern: For n=3 we used lines: y=x (slope 1), x+2y=5 (slope -1/2), 2x+y=5 (slope -2). These lines each contain one hypotenuse point. For n=4, we need 4 lines, each containing one of the points (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). We also need to cover other points. Could we use lines that are "reflections" of each other? For instance, take lines through (1,4) and (2,2): slope (2-4)/(2-1)=-2, line: 2x+y=6? Actually (1,4): 2+4=6; (2,2):4+2=6; yes. That line also passes through (3,0) not needed; (4,-2) no. So it covers (1,4) and (2,2). Then we need to cover (2,3),(3,2),(4,1) and the rest. Let's try to assign lines: L1 through (1,4) and (2,2) (slope -2). L2 through (2,3) and (3,1): slope (1-3)/(3-2)=-2, line: 2x+y=7? Check (2,3):4+3=7; (3,1):6+1=7; yes. That covers (2,3) and (3,1). L3 through (3,2) and (4,1): slope (1-2)/(4-3)=-1, not sunny. So not. Could use line through (3,2) and (2,1): slope (1-2)/(2-3)= -1/-1=1, line: y=x+1? Actually (2,1):1=2-1? Let's compute: y-2 = 1*(x-3) => y=x-1; check (2,1):1=2-1 yes; (3,2):2=3-1 yes. That line is sunny (slope 1). It covers (2,1) and (3,2). But (2,1) is not a hypotenuse point; it's interior. And (3,2) is a hypotenuse point. So L3: y=x-1, passes through (2,1) and (3,2). Then L4: through (4,1) and (3,? ) maybe (4,1) and (2,2)? slope (2-1)/(2-4)=1/(-2)=-1/2, line: x+2y=6? Check (4,1):4+2=6; (2,2):2+4=6 yes. That's sunny (slope -1/2). It covers (4,1) and (2,2). But (2,2) already covered by L1? L1: 2x+y=6 contains (2,2) yes. So that's fine. Now we have covered points: L1: (1,4),(2,2); L2: (2,3),(3,1); L3: (2,1),(3,2); L4: (4,1),(2,2). So we have covered: (1,4),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(2,1),(3,2),(4,1). That's 7 points. What about (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,? we already have (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) covered; (3,? we have (3,1),(3,2) covered; (4,? we have (4,1) covered; (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) left. And also (2,? all done; (3,? done; (4,1) done; (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) need to be covered. Could we cover them with some of the existing lines? L1: 2x+y=6, for x=1:2+y=6 => y=4, so (1,4) only; (1,2):2+2=4 not 6; (1,1):2+1=3 not 6. L2: 2x+y=7, for x=1:2+y=7 => y=5 not in set; (1,2):2+2=4 not 7; (1,1):2+1=3 not 7. L3: y=x-1, for x=1:1-1=0 not; (1,2):2=0? no; (1,3):3=0? no. L4: x+2y=6, for x=1:1+2y=6 => 2y=5 => y=2.5 no; (1,2):1+4=5 not 6; (1,3):1+6=7 not 6. So these points are not covered. So we need to cover (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) with additional lines, but we already used 4 lines; we have n=4 lines total. So we need to choose the 4 lines differently. Perhaps we can have a line that covers multiple of these points. For instance, a horizontal line y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). That would cover (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). But that line is not sunny (horizontal). So if we use that, we have one non-sunny line, and we need 3 sunny lines to cover the rest. For n=4, we can try to have k=4 sunny? That would require no horizontal/vertical/slope -1 lines, so we need to cover (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) with sunny lines. But can a sunny line cover (1,1),(1,2),(1,3)? That would be vertical line x=1, which is not sunny. So no. Could a sunny line cover (1,1) and (1,2)? That would be vertical again. So each sunny line can contain at most one point with a given x-coordinate? Actually, if a line is not vertical, it can intersect a vertical line at most once, so it cannot contain two points with the same x-coordinate unless it is vertical. So a sunny line cannot contain two points with the same a (x-coordinate). Similarly, it cannot contain two points with the same b (y-coordinate) unless it is horizontal. So a sunny line can contain at most one point from each column (fixed x) and each row (fixed y). So for points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3), they all have x=1, so they are in the same column. A sunny line cannot contain more than one of them. So to cover all three, we would need at least three sunny lines, each covering one of them. But we have only 4 lines total; if we use three sunny lines to cover the column, we have one line left to cover the rest of the points. But we also need to cover the other points. So perhaps it's still possible if we assign the lines carefully.

But note: The points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) are all on the left boundary (a=1). They must be covered by lines. Since sunny lines cannot contain two points with same x, we need at least three sunny lines to cover these three points. That would give at least three sunny lines. Then we have one line left; but that one line must cover all the remaining points not covered by these three lines. The remaining points are many. But we have only one line left; can it cover all the remaining points? Possibly if that line is horizontal (y=1) or vertical (x=1) or slope -1, but those are non-sunny. But we are aiming for k=4 sunny lines, so all lines must be sunny, so we cannot have a non-sunny line. So the line that covers the remaining points must be sunny. But can a single sunny line cover all the points not covered by the three sunny lines? That seems unlikely because the remaining points include points with different x and y. But maybe the three sunny lines can cover many points, and the fourth sunny line can cover the rest. Let's attempt to construct.

We need to cover the triangle with 4 sunny lines. Let's denote the set of points. Let's try to assign each of the three left column points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) to three different sunny lines. So we have lines L1, L2, L3 each containing one of these points. They can also contain other points. Then L4 is the fourth sunny line. We need to cover all points. Let's try to see if we can cover (1,4) as well? (1,4) is on the hypotenuse. That must be covered by some line. Possibly by L4 or by one of L1,L2,L3. But note: a sunny line cannot contain two points with the same x, so L1 already contains (1,1); if it also contains (1,4), that would be two points with x=1, which is not allowed unless the line is vertical, but vertical is not sunny. So L1 cannot contain (1,4). Similarly L2 cannot contain (1,4) because it already has (1,2); L3 cannot contain (1,4) because it has (1,3). So (1,4) must be covered by L4. So L4 passes through (1,4). Also, (2,3),(3,2),(4,1) are hypotenuse points; they must be covered. Possibly by L4 or by L1,L2,L3. Since L1,L2,L3 cannot contain two points with same x, they could contain at most one of these hypotenuse points, but they might contain them. However, they already have a point with x=1; if they also contain a point with x=2, that's fine because x differs. So we could assign some hypotenuse points to L1,L2,L3. But note: A sunny line can contain at most one point from each column; so it can contain one point from x=1, one from x=2, etc. So L1 could contain (1,1) and maybe (2,?) etc. Similarly, L2 and L3. So we need to cover all points. Let's attempt systematic construction.

We have points: P = {(1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1)}. We need 4 sunny lines. Let's try to use lines that are "diagonals" with slopes like 1, 2, 1/2, etc. We can try to assign each of the three left column points to a line that also passes through some other points to cover many. For instance, consider line through (1,1) and (2,2): y=x. This covers (1,1) and (2,2). Also could cover (3,3) not in set; (4,4) no. So it covers two points. That's fine. But we need to cover (1,2),(1,3),(1,4) and the rest. Maybe we can use line through (1,2) and (2,3): slope 1, line y=x+1? That would be (1,2):2=1+1; (2,3):3=2+1; yes. That covers (1,2) and (2,3). But (2,3) is hypotenuse? Actually (2,3) sum=5, which is n+1=5? For n=4, n+1=5, so (2,3) is on hypotenuse. So that line would cover (1,2) and (2,3). Then we need to cover (1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Could we use line through (1,3) and (2,1): slope (1-3)/(2-1) = -2, line: 2x+y=5? Check (1,3):2+3=5; (2,1):4+1=5; yes. That covers (1,3) and (2,1). Then we need to cover (1,4),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). And we have one line left. Can we cover all these with one sunny line? For instance, line through (1,4) and (3,1): slope (1-4)/(3-1) = -3/2, line: y-4 = -3/2(x-1) => 2y-8 = -3x+3 => 3x+2y=11. Check (4,1):12+2=14 not 11; (3,2):9+4=13 not 11; (4,1) not. Could use line through (1,4) and (4,1): slope (1-4)/(4-1) = -3/3 = -1, that's slope -1, not sunny. So not. Could use line through (3,2) and (4,1): slope -1, not sunny. So maybe the fourth line can be a line through (1,4) and (3,2): slope (2-4)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1, not sunny. So not. Could use line through (3,1) and (4,1): horizontal y=1, not sunny. So it seems we cannot cover all remaining points with a single sunny line. Perhaps we need to adjust assignments.

Maybe we can use a line that is not through a left column point but covers multiple hypotenuse points? But a sunny line cannot contain two hypotenuse points because that would force it to be the hypotenuse line (slope -1) which is not sunny. So at most one hypotenuse point per sunny line. So we have 4 hypotenuse points; to cover them, we need at least 4 lines (since each line can cover at most one). But we have exactly 4 lines total. So each line must cover exactly one hypotenuse point. That is a necessary condition if all lines are sunny. So we must assign each of the 4 hypotenuse points to a distinct line. So we have lines L_i each containing one of (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Then we also need to cover the other points. So each line must also contain some other points (maybe multiple) to cover the rest. But the lines cannot contain two points with same x or same y (unless horizontal/vertical). So they can contain at most one point from each column and each row. So we have to cover all points.

Now, the points in column a=1 are (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). (1,4) is on hypotenuse and must be covered by some line, say L4. So L4 contains (1,4). The other three points in column a=1 must be covered by lines L1,L2,L3. But each of those lines already contains one hypotenuse point: L1 contains (2,3) or (3,2) or (4,1). But they cannot contain two points with same x, so they can contain at most one point from column a=1. So we need to assign each of (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) to one of L1,L2,L3. That is possible. Then each of L1,L2,L3 also contains a hypotenuse point (from the set { (2,3),(3,2),(4,1) } but note: (4,1) has x=4, so it's fine. So we have L1: contains (1,x) and (2,3) or something; L2: contains (1,y) and (3,2) or something; L3: contains (1,z) and (4,1) or something. But we also need to cover points in other columns: (2,1),(2,2),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1) and also (3,2) is hypotenuse, (2,3) is hypotenuse, (4,1) is hypotenuse, (1,4) is hypotenuse. The other points: (2,1),(2,2),(3,1) are interior. They must be covered by some lines. Since each line can contain at most one point from each column, we need to ensure that all these points are covered by the lines.

We have four lines: L1,L2,L3,L4. Each line can contain points from multiple columns. We need to cover the points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) are assigned to L1,L2,L3 (one each). (1,4) is assigned to L4. (2,3) is assigned to one line, say L1. (3,2) assigned to L2, (4,1) assigned to L3, and (2,1),(2,2),(3,1) left. Also, we have (2,2) and (2,1) and (3,1) need to be covered. Possibly they can be covered by the same lines that already contain points from column 1 and hypotenuse points. For instance, L1 contains (1,1) and (2,3). Could it also contain (2,1)? That would be two points with x=2, which is not allowed because that would require the line to be vertical (since two points with same x are on a vertical line). But L1 is not vertical, so it cannot contain both (2,3) and (2,1). So (2,1) cannot be on L1 if (2,3) is. Similarly, (2,2) cannot be on L1 if (2,3) is. So (2,1) and (2,2) must be covered by other lines. L4 contains (1,4); it could also contain (2,?) but it already has a point with x=1; if it also contains (2,?), that's fine, but it cannot contain two points with same x. So L4 could contain (2,2) or (2,1) but not both. Similarly, L2 and L3 could contain some of these points. We need to cover all.

Let's attempt to find a concrete assignment.

We have hypotenuse points: H1=(1,4), H2=(2,3), H3=(3,2), H4=(4,1). We need lines L1,...,L4 each containing exactly one H_i. Let's assign L1: contains H2=(2,3). L2: contains H3=(3,2). L3: contains H4=(4,1). L4: contains H1=(1,4). Now, we also need to cover points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3). We can assign them to L1,L2,L3 respectively (since L4 already has H1, but could also cover (1,1)? But that would be two points with x=1, not allowed because L4 is not vertical; so L4 cannot contain any other point with x=1. So (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) must be on L1,L2,L3. So let's decide: L1 contains (2,3) and (1,1). L2 contains (3,2) and (1,2). L3 contains (4,1) and (1,3). Now, we need to cover the remaining interior points: (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Also, note that (2,2) is interior; (2,1) interior; (3,1) interior. Also, (2,2) and (2,1) have x=2; they cannot be on L1 because L1 already has (2,3) and cannot have another x=2. So they must be on L2,L3,L4. Similarly, (3,1) has x=3; L2 already has (3,2); cannot have another x=3; so it must be on L1,L3,L4. (2,1) and (2,2) have x=2; L1 cannot, so L2,L3,L4. L2 contains (3,2); it could also contain (2,2) or (2,1) if the line passes through those. But L2 already contains (3,2) and (1,2). The line through (1,2) and (3,2) is horizontal? Actually (1,2) and (3,2) have same y, so line is y=2, which is horizontal; but that line is not sunny. However, we assigned L2 to contain (1,2) and (3,2); that line is y=2, which is horizontal, so it's not sunny. But we need all lines to be sunny. So this assignment fails because L2 would be horizontal. So we must ensure that lines are not horizontal/vertical/slope -1. So when we assign two points to a line, the line must be sunny. So we cannot have L2 containing (1,2) and (3,2) because that's horizontal. So we need to assign points to lines such that each line is sunny. That imposes restrictions: The two points assigned to a line must not be horizontal (i.e., same y) unless the line is horizontal, which is not sunny; similarly not vertical; and not slope -1. So we need to choose points that are not collinear in those directions.

So we need to assign each of the three left column points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) to one of the lines L1,L2,L3, but also ensure that the line through the hypotenuse point and that left column point is sunny. That means the slope must not be 0, ∞, or -1. So for each pair (H_i, (1,j)), we need the slope to be not 0, ∞, or -1. Let's compute slopes for each possible pairing:

- H2=(2,3) with (1,1): slope = (1-3)/(1-2) = (-2)/(-1)=2, which is sunny (since slope 2 not 0,∞,-1). So OK.
- H2 with (1,2): slope = (2-3)/(1-2) = (-1)/(-1)=1, sunny (slope 1, not -1,0,∞). OK.
- H2 with (1,3): slope = (3-3)/(1-2)=0/(-1)=0, horizontal, not sunny. So H2 cannot pair with (1,3) if we want sunny line.
- H3=(3,2) with (1,1): slope = (1-2)/(1-3) = (-1)/(-2)=1/2, sunny.
- H3 with (1,2): slope = (2-2)/(1-3)=0/(-2)=0, horizontal, not sunny.
- H3 with (1,3): slope = (3-2)/(1-3)=1/(-2)=-1/2, sunny (slope -1/2).
- H4=(4,1) with (1,1): slope = (1-1)/(1-4)=0/(-3)=0, horizontal, not sunny.
- H4 with (1,2): slope = (2-1)/(1-4)=1/(-3)=-1/3, sunny.
- H4 with (1,3): slope = (3-1)/(1-4)=2/(-3)=-2/3, sunny.

So the allowed pairings: For H2, allowed left points: (1,1) and (1,2); not (1,3). For H3, allowed: (1,1) and (1,3); not (1,2). For H4, allowed: (1,2) and (1,3); not (1,1). So we have three left points and three hypotenuse points, and we need to assign each left point to a distinct hypotenuse point such that the pairing is allowed. This is like a bipartite matching. The allowed assignments: H2 can take (1,1) or (1,2); H3 can take (1,1) or (1,3); H4 can take (1,2) or (1,3). We need to assign each of the three left points to one of the hypotenuse points. Is there a perfect matching? Let's list possibilities: If we assign (1,1) to H2, then (1,2) could go to H4, (1,3) to H3? Check H4 with (1,2) is allowed, H3 with (1,3) is allowed. So that works: L1: H2-(1,1); L2: H3-(1,3); L3: H4-(1,2). But then we also have H1=(1,4) assigned to L4; L4 contains (1,4) and maybe also some other points? But L4 must be sunny; it could contain other points, but we need to cover the remaining interior points (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Let's see if we can cover them with these lines.

So we have lines:
L1: through H2=(2,3) and (1,1). Equation: slope =2, line: y-3 = 2(x-2) => y=2x-1? Check (1,1):1=2-1=1 yes. So L1: y=2x-1. This line contains (1,1) and (2,3). Also (3,5) not needed; (0,-1) no. So covers points: (1,1) and (2,3). Also could cover (3,5) not in set; (4,7) no.
L2: through H3=(3,2) and (1,3). Slope = (1-2)/(1-3) = (-1)/(-2)=1/2? Actually we need to compute line through (3,2) and (1,3): slope = (3-2)/(1-3)=1/(-2)=-1/2. So slope -1/2. Equation: y-2 = -1/2(x-3) => y = -1/2 x + 3/2 + 2 = -1/2 x + 7/2? Let's compute: y = -1/2 x + 7/2. Check (1,3): -1/2+7/2=6/2=3 yes. So L2: y = -1/2 x + 7/2. This line contains (1,3) and (3,2). Also (5,1) not in set? (5,1): -5/2+7/2=2/2=1 yes, but (5,1) sum=6>5, not required. So covers (1,3) and (3,2).
L3: through H4=(4,1) and (1,2). Slope = (2-1)/(1-4)=1/(-3)=-1/3. Equation: y-1 = -1/3(x-4) => y = -1/3 x + 4/3 + 1 = -1/3 x + 7/3. Check (1,2): -1/3+7/3=6/3=2 yes. So L3: y = -1/3 x + 7/3. Contains (4,1) and (1,2).
L4: through H1=(1,4). Need to be sunny, so it can contain other points, but must be non-horizontal/vertical/slope -1. It could contain some of the remaining interior points. We need to cover (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Also note that (2,1) and (2,2) have x=2; (3,1) has x=3. Can we assign them to L4? L4 contains (1,4). We need to choose a line through (1,4) that is sunny and contains as many of these points as possible. But we only have L4 as the fourth line; it must cover all remaining points. So we need to find a line L4 (sunny) that contains (1,4) and also contains (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). However, can a single line contain three points with different x? Possibly, if they are collinear. Are (1,4),(2,1),(3,? ) collinear? Slope between (1,4) and (2,1) is (1-4)/(2-1)=-3, so line: y-4 = -3(x-1) => y = -3x+7. For x=3, y = -9+7=-2, not 1. So (3,1) not on it. Could we have line through (1,4) and (2,2): slope (2-4)/(2-1)=-2, line: y=-2x+6; for x=3, y=-6+6=0, not 1. So not. Could have line through (1,4) and (3,1): slope (1-4)/(3-1)=-3/2, line: y = -3/2 x + 11/2; for x=2, y=-3+5.5=2.5 not 1 or 2. So no line contains all three. So we need to cover (2,1),(2,2),(3,1) with lines that already exist? But we already used L1,L2,L3; they only cover two points each, and none of the remaining points are on those lines (check: L1: y=2x-1, for x=2 gives y=3, so (2,3) covered; x=3 gives y=5 not; x=1 gives y=1; so (2,1) not; (2,2) not; (3,1) not. L2: y=-1/2x+7/2, x=2 gives y=-1+3.5=2.5 no; x=3 gives y=-1.5+3.5=2, so (3,2) covered; x=1 gives y=3; so (2,1) not; (2,2) not; (3,1) not. L3: y=-1/3x+7/3, x=2 gives y=-2/3+7/3=5/3≈1.666 not; x=3 gives y=-1+7/3=4/3≈1.333; x=1 gives y=2; so none. So indeed the remaining points are not covered. So with this assignment, we have only L4 left; but L4 cannot cover all three. Could we have L4 cover two of them, and the third is covered by one of the other lines? But L1,L2,L3 are already fixed; they only cover two points each, and none of the remaining points lie on them. So no. So we need to adjust the assignment of left points to hypotenuse points, or maybe use a different line for H1? But H1=(1,4) is on the hypotenuse; it must be covered by some line. If we don't assign it to L4, we could assign it to L1,L2,L3. But L1,L2,L3 already contain a hypotenuse point each; we could instead have one of them contain H1 and another left point, and then assign the other hypotenuse points to the remaining lines. But we have 4 lines; each must contain exactly one hypotenuse point because we have 4 hypotenuse points and each sunny line can contain at most one. So we need a bijection from lines to hypotenuse points. So we can choose which line gets which H_i. We already assigned L1:H2, L2:H3, L3:H4, L4:H1. Could we assign L1:H1, L2:H2, L3:H3, L4:H4? Then we would have to assign left points to L1,L2,L3 (since L4 already has H4). Then we need to match left points to H1, H2, H3 respectively. Let's see allowed pairings: For H1=(1,4) with left points: H1 with (1,1): slope = (1-4)/(1-1) undefined (vertical), not sunny; H1 with (1,2): slope = (2-4)/(1-1)= -2/0 undefined; H1 with (1,3): slope = (3-4)/(1-1) undefined; actually any pair with same x=1 gives vertical line, which is not sunny. So H1 cannot pair with any left point because that would give vertical line. So H1 must be assigned to a line that does not contain any other point with x=1? But we need to cover left points; if H1 is on a line that also contains a left point, that line would be vertical, not sunny. So H1 cannot be paired with a left point if we want sunny line. However, H1 can be on a line that does not contain any left point, but then that line must contain some other points to cover the left points. But we have 4 lines; if H1 is on a line that does not contain any left point, then the other three lines must cover the three left points. But they also contain H2,H3,H4 each. So we need to assign each left point to one of the lines L2,L3,L4 (if L1 is H1). But we also need to cover the interior points. So we need to consider all possibilities.

Let's systematically analyze the matching constraints for n=4.

We have points P. We need to cover them with 4 lines L1,...,L4, all sunny. For each line, it contains a set of points from P. Since lines are distinct and sunny, they have slopes not 0,∞,-1.

We need to cover all 10 points. There are 4 lines, each can contain many points. But we also have the condition that the points on the hypotenuse (a+b=5) are H1=(1,4), H2=(2,3), H3=(3,2), H4=(4,1). Each sunny line can contain at most one of these points. Since there are 4 lines and 4 hypotenuse points, each line must contain exactly one hypotenuse point. So we have a bijection between lines and hypotenuse points.

Now, the left column points (a=1) are L1'=(1,1), L2'=(1,2), L3'=(1,3), L4'=(1,4). Note that (1,4) is a hypotenuse point. So the three left points (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) must be covered by the lines that do not contain (1,4) (since that line cannot contain any other point with x=1 because it would be vertical, which is not sunny). So they must be covered by the other three lines (which contain H2,H3,H4). Each of those lines already contains a hypotenuse point; they can also contain at most one point from column a=1 (since if they contain two points with x=1, they would be vertical, not sunny). So we need to assign each of (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) to a distinct line among the three lines containing H2,H3,H4. So that's a matching.

Similarly, the bottom row points (b=1) are (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). (4,1) is a hypotenuse point. So the points (1,1),(2,1),(3,1) must be covered by lines that are not vertical? Actually, they have different x; a sunny line can contain at most one of these? It can contain at most one point with a given x, but it can contain points with different x. So we need to assign these points to lines that can cover them. Also, the point (2,2) and (3,2) etc. We need to cover all points.

We can try to find a valid configuration by considering possible slopes.

Let's denote the three lines (not containing H1) as L2 (contains H2), L3 (contains H3), L4 (contains H4). For each, we assign one of the left points (1,j) with j=1,2,3, and we need the line through H_i and that left point to be sunny. That's the allowed pairings we computed. So we need a perfect matching between {H2,H3,H4} and {L1',L2',L3'} (where L1'=(1,1), L2'=(1,2), L3'=(1,3)). From the earlier allowed pairings, we have:

- H2 can pair with (1,1) or (1,2); not (1,3).
- H3 can pair with (1,1) or (1,3); not (1,2).
- H4 can pair with (1,2) or (1,3); not (1,1).

So we need a matching. There are possible matchings: For example, H2 with (1,1), H3 with (1,3), H4 with (1,2) — that's a matching. Also H2 with (1,2), H3 with (1,1), H4 with (1,3) — that's another. Then we have L1 through H1=(1,4) and must be sunny. It could also contain other points. We need to cover all points not already covered by these lines. Let's compute the points covered by L2,L3,L4 for each matching.

Could some left point be covered by L1 (the line through H1) if L1 is not vertical? But L1 must contain H1=(1,4). If it also contains (1,1), that would be vertical line x=1, which is not sunny. So L1 cannot contain any other point with x=1. So (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) must be covered by lines L2,L3,L4. So they need to be assigned to those lines. That seems necessary.

But maybe we could have a line that contains H1 and also contains (1,1) if the line is not vertical? But two points with same x determine a vertical line. So no. So indeed, the three left points must be on L2,L3,L4.

Thus, the only way to have all 4 lines sunny is to have a perfect matching between {H2,H3,H4} and { (1,1),(1,2),(1,3) } such that each line is sunny. We tried the two matchings; both failed to cover the remaining interior points. Could there be a different assignment where the lines L2,L3,L4 are not simply the lines through H_i and a left point? They could contain additional points beyond the two we assigned. But we assigned exactly one left point to each; we could also assign that the line through H_i and that left point might also contain some of the interior points (like (2,1) or (2,2) etc). In the matchings we computed, the lines are determined by two points; they may contain other lattice points within the triangle. For instance, L2 in M2: y=x+1 contains points (3,4) not in set; (0,1) no; (2,3) already; (1,2) already; (4,5) no; so only those two. Maybe we could choose a different matching: For H2, we could assign it to (1,1) or (1,2); for H3, to (1,1) or (1,3); for H4, to (1,2) or (1,3). The matchings we considered are the only two perfect matchings. But note: We could also have the possibility that one of the lines L2,L3,L4 contains not only the hypotenuse point and a left point, but also maybe another point from the left column? But that would be vertical, not allowed. So no.

Thus, n=4 with all 4 sunny seems impossible. But we need to verify if maybe we can use a line that contains H1 and also some other point that is not a left point, but then the left points might be covered by L2,L3,L4 without requiring each line to contain a left point? Actually, we need to cover left points; they must be on some line. The only lines that can cover them are L2,L3,L4 (since L1 cannot because it's vertical if it contains another x=1 point, but could L1 contain a left point if it is not vertical? As argued, any two points with same x determine a vertical line. So L1 cannot contain (1,1) unless L1 is vertical, which is not sunny. So indeed left points must be on L2,L3,L4. So each of the three left points must be on at least one of those three lines.
