So we have a bijection. So our matchings are forced.

Now, we need to cover the remaining points: (1,4) is already on L1; (2,1),(2,2),(3,1) left. Could it be that some of these points are already on L2,L3,L4? We already checked; they are not. So they must be on L1. But L1 is a line through (1,4). Can it contain all three? Possibly if we choose L1 not as the line through (1,4) and some other point, but maybe L1 could contain (2,1) and (2,2)? That would be vertical line x=2, not sunny. So no. Could L1 contain (2,1) and (3,1)? That would be horizontal y=1, not sunny. So no. Could L1 contain (2,1) and (2,2)? vertical. So the only possibility is that some of the interior points are covered by L2,L3,L4 if we choose different assignments? But in the matchings we used, the lines L2,L3,L4 are determined by two points each; they may contain additional points beyond those two if we choose the matching differently? Let's list all possible lines that go through a given H_i and a left point. For H2, with (1,1) gives line L2: y=2x-1; with (1,2) gives L2: y=x+1. For H3, with (1,1) gives line: y=0.5x+0.5; with (1,3) gives line: y=-0.5x+3.5? Actually we computed: through (3,2) and (1,3): slope -1/2, line: y = -1/2 x + 7/2? Check: (1,3): -0.5+3.5=3 yes. So L3: y = -0.5x+3.5. For H4, with (1,2) gives line: y = -1/3 x + 7/3; with (1,3) gives line: y = -2/3 x + 11/3.

Now, we need to cover the interior points (2,1),(2,2),(3,1). Let's see if any of these points lie on any of these lines:

- For H2 with (1,1): line y=2x-1. (2,1): y=3 no; (2,2): y=3 no; (3,1): y=5 no.
- For H2 with (1,2): line y=x+1. (2,1): y=3 no; (2,2): y=3 no; (3,1): y=4 no.

So none of these lines contain any of the interior points. Then the interior points must be covered by L1. But L1 cannot cover all three because they are not collinear with (1,4). So n=4 with all 4 sunny is impossible.

But could we have a configuration where L1 (through H1) also contains one of the left points? That would be vertical, not sunny. So no.

Thus, k=4 is impossible for n=4.

What about k=3 for n=4? That means exactly 3 sunny lines, 1 non-sunny line. We can use a non-sunny line to cover many points, e.g., a horizontal line y=1 covers (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). That's 4 points. Then we need 3 sunny lines to cover the rest: points with b≥2, and also maybe (2,2),(2,3),(3,2),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1) is covered by y=1? Actually (4,1) is covered by y=1; (1,1) also; (2,1); (3,1). So remaining points: (1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,2),(2,3),(3,2). That's 6 points. Need to cover them with 3 sunny lines. Is that possible? Likely yes. For n=4, we can try to construct.

Alternatively, use a vertical line x=1 covering (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4). That's 4 points. Then remaining: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1) — 6 points. Need 3 sunny lines. Possibly.

Use line x+y=5 (hypotenuse) covering (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's 4 points. Then remaining: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1) — 6 points. Need 3 sunny lines. Possibly.

We need to check if there exists a configuration with exactly k sunny lines for any k from 0 to n. Probably the answer is: all k from 0 to n-2? Or something like that. Let's try to determine for general n.

We have triangle T_n. We need to cover all points. Let's denote the set of points on the hypotenuse: H = {(a, n+1-a): a=1,...,n}. There are n points. Each sunny line can contain at most one of these points. So if we have s sunny lines, they can cover at most s points from H. The remaining n-s points from H must be covered by non-sunny lines. A non-sunny line can be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. The only non-sunny line that can cover more than one point from H is the hypotenuse line itself (x+y=n+1). A horizontal line y=c can cover at most one point from H (since b varies, only one point with that y). Similarly vertical. So if we want to cover all n hypotenuse points, we need at least n-s non-sunny lines that are the hypotenuse line? But we have only n total lines. So we need to cover the hypotenuse points with lines. There are two extremes: Either use the line x+y=n+1, which is non-sunny, and then it covers all n hypotenuse points with one line. Then we have n-1 lines left to cover the rest of the points (with sum ≤ n). That's a triangle of size n-1. Or we can cover the hypotenuse points using n lines (each covering one point), which could be any lines (sunny or not). But if we use n lines, we have no lines left for other points, so each line must also cover some other points. In that case, all n lines are used for the hypotenuse points, and each must be sunny? Not necessarily; they could be non-sunny, but then the number of sunny lines would be less than n. So we need to consider both strategies.

The problem asks: For given n, determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying the covering condition and exactly k of them are sunny. So we need to characterize possible k.

Observation: The covering condition is quite strong. It might be that the only possible configurations are those where the lines are either all horizontal (y=1,...,n) giving k=0, or all vertical (x=1,...,n) giving k=0, or all slope -1 (x+y=2,...,n+1) giving k=0, or some mix. But we already found for n=3 a configuration with all 3 sunny lines. So k=3 works for n=3. For n=4, we suspect k=4 is impossible, but maybe k=3 works, and k=2,1,0 work. Let's test n=4 with k=3: we need 3 sunny lines and 1 non-sunny line. Can we construct? Let's try to use the non-sunny line as the hypotenuse line x+y=5. That covers (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Then we have 3 sunny lines to cover the remaining points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(2,2),(3,1). That's 6 points. Need 3 sunny lines covering all 6 points. Each sunny line can contain at most one of the points on the "left boundary" (a=1) and bottom boundary (b=1)? Actually, we need to cover these points. Since we have 3 lines, they need to cover 6 points. Each line could cover 2 points on average. That's plausible. We need to find 3 sunny lines such that their union contains these 6 points. Also, the lines must be distinct and sunny. Let's attempt.

Now, we need to prove that k=2 is impossible. Let's attempt a general proof.

We have n distinct lines L_1,...,L_n. Exactly k of them are sunny. We need to cover all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1.

We already know that k=0 works (all horizontal lines). So we need to show that if k=2, then it's impossible. Similarly, if k≥4? But we only need to determine all k that can occur; we have shown that k=0,1,3 work. We need to show that any other k is impossible. For n=3, k=2 fails; for n=4, k=2 fails; for n=5, k=2 fails; so likely k=2 always fails. What about k=4? For n=4, k=4 fails; for n=5, k=4 fails; so likely k≥4 also fails for n≥4? But we need to show that if k≥4, then it's impossible. However, we have k=3 for n=4,5,6,7,... so k=3 is possible. So the only possible k are 0,1,3. But we need to prove that no other k works.

We can attempt to prove an upper bound on the number of sunny lines based on the covering requirement. For a given n, the points on the hypotenuse are n points, each must be covered by a line. A sunny line can cover at most one hypotenuse point (since if a sunny line covered two hypotenuse points, it would be the hypotenuse line, which is not sunny). So the number of sunny lines s = k must be at least the number of hypotenuse points that are covered by sunny lines. But since we have n lines total, the remaining n-s lines are non-sunny, and they can cover the remaining hypotenuse points. However, we need to cover all points, not just hypotenuse. So perhaps we can derive that s cannot be 2 because the non-sunny lines cannot cover all points? We need a rigorous argument.

One approach: Consider the set of points with a+b = n+1 (hypotenuse). There are n such points. Each line that is not parallel to x+y=0 can intersect the hypotenuse at at most one point. So any line that is sunny cannot contain two hypotenuse points; a line that is parallel to x+y=0 (slope -1) can contain at most one hypotenuse point if it's not the hypotenuse itself? Actually, if a line is parallel to x+y=0, it's of form x+y = d. For d = n+1, it contains all hypotenuse points; for d ≠ n+1, it contains no hypotenuse points. So the only non-sunny line that can contain multiple hypotenuse points is the hypotenuse itself (x+y=n+1). So if we have s sunny lines, they cover at most s hypotenuse points. The remaining n-s hypotenuse points must be covered by non-sunny lines. But the non-sunny lines can be of three types: horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. A horizontal line y=c can contain at most one hypotenuse point (since only one point on hypotenuse has that b). Similarly vertical. A slope -1 line can contain either all n hypotenuse points if it's the hypotenuse, or none otherwise. So to cover the remaining n-s hypotenuse points, we need at least n-s non-sunny lines that are not slope -1? Or we can use the hypotenuse line to cover all n points, in which case we need only 1 non-sunny line to cover all hypotenuse points. So the number of non-sunny lines can be as low as 1 if we use the hypotenuse line. That's allowed. So the number of sunny lines can be up to n (if we use only sunny lines). But we already have a construction with 3 sunny lines for any n, so the maximum s is at least 3. So s can be 0,1,3. Could s be 2? Let's try to see if there is any parity or other constraint that prevents s=2.

We can consider the following: For each line, count the number of points from P that lie on it. The sum over all lines of these counts is at least |P| = n(n+1)/2. Since lines are distinct, we can try to find an upper bound on the sum of counts for non-sunny lines, and then see if with s=2 sunny lines, the total sum cannot reach n(n+1)/2. We need to show that if s=2, then the sum of counts is at most something less than n(n+1)/2.

We need to consider the maximum number of points a line can contain from P. A line that is not sunny can contain many points. For example, a horizontal line y=c can contain points with a from 1 to n+1-c, so number = n+1-c. For c=1, that's n; for c=2, that's n-1; etc. A vertical line x=c gives n+1-c points as well. The hypotenuse line x+y=n+1 gives n points. So the maximum points on a non-sunny line is n (if it's y=1 or x=1 or x+y=n+1). So each non-sunny line can cover up to n points. However, these lines can overlap in coverage; the sum of counts can be larger than |P| because points can be covered multiple times. But we need a lower bound on the sum of counts: it must be at least |P|. Since we have n lines, and each line can cover at most something, we can get an upper bound on the sum of counts if we can show that the points covered by each line are limited in a way that the total sum cannot be too large. Now, we need to show that k=2 is impossible. Let's attempt a general proof for n≥3.

Assume we have n lines covering all points (a,b) with a,b positive integers and a+b ≤ n+1. Exactly k of these lines are sunny. We want to show that if k=2, then it's impossible.

We can use the following approach: Consider the points on the hypotenuse H = {(1,n), (2,n-1), ..., (n,1)}. There are n points. Each line that is sunny can contain at most one of these points. The non-sunny lines can contain multiple hypotenuse points, but only the line x+y=n+1 can contain all n points. If we use that line as a non-sunny line, then we can cover all hypotenuse points with one line. Otherwise, we need at least n non-sunny lines to cover the n hypotenuse points (since each non-sunny line can contain at most one hypotenuse point if it's not the hypotenuse itself). So the number of non-sunny lines must be at least n if we don't use the hypotenuse line. But we have n lines total, so if we don't use the hypotenuse line, then all lines are non-sunny (since s=2 sunny lines, so non-sunny = n-2). That would give n-2 lines to cover n hypotenuse points, but each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 hypotenuse point (if it's horizontal or vertical) or possibly many if it's the hypotenuse. But if we don't use the hypotenuse line, then each non-sunny line can cover at most 1 hypotenuse point. So we would need at least n non-sunny lines to cover n points, but we have only n-2 non-sunny lines. Therefore, it's impossible to cover all hypotenuse points if we don't use the hypotenuse line. Thus, we must use the hypotenuse line x+y=n+1 as one of the non-sunny lines. So let L_h: x+y=n+1 be one of the lines. Since we have exactly 2 sunny lines, the remaining n-1 lines are non-sunny. One of them is the hypotenuse. The other n-2 non-sunny lines are either horizontal or vertical (since they are not sunny, they are parallel to x-axis, y-axis, or x+y=0; but the only slope -1 line we use is the hypotenuse, so others are horizontal or vertical). So we have n-2 horizontal or vertical lines, plus the hypotenuse line. Now, these n-2 lines can cover some of the boundary points.

Consider the set of points with a=1 (the left column). There are n points: (1,1), (1,2), ..., (1,n). The line x=1 is vertical; if we have x=1 as a non-sunny line, it covers all these points. If we don't have x=1, then each of these points must be covered by some other line. A sunny line can contain at most one point from this column (since it's not vertical). A horizontal line y=c can contain at most one point from this column (the point (1,c) if c is between 1 and n). So to cover all n points in the left column, we would need at least n lines that each cover at most one point, which is impossible if we have only 2 sunny lines and n-2 other lines. But note: The hypotenuse line x+y=n+1 contains (1,n) only; it covers one point. So we could cover (1,n) with the hypotenuse. Then we need to cover the remaining n-1 points in the left column. Each of these must be covered by a line that is either sunny, horizontal, or vertical. But a sunny line can cover at most one of them, and a horizontal line can cover at most one of them (since it's y=c, it covers (1,c) only). A vertical line x=1 would cover all of them, but that's a single line. So if we have x=1 as a non-sunny line, it covers all left column points. So we need to include x=1 as one of the non-sunny lines. Similarly, consider the bottom row b=1: points (1,1), (2,1), ..., (n,1). The line y=1 is horizontal and covers all these points. So we need to include y=1 as a non-sunny line to cover the bottom row. The hypotenuse covers (n,1). So we need to cover the other points. So to cover all points, we likely need to have both x=1 and y=1 as non-sunny lines. But we have n-2 non-sunny lines besides the hypotenuse; we can include x=1 and y=1 as two of them. Then we have n-4 other non-sunny lines that can be chosen arbitrarily (horizontal or vertical). Now, after using x=1, y=1, and the hypotenuse, what points are left? Points with a≥2, b≥2, and a+b ≤ n (since points with a+b=n+1 are covered by hypotenuse, points with a=1 or b=1 are covered by x=1 and y=1). So the uncovered points are exactly the triangle of points with a≥2,b≥2,a+b≤n. Now, we have only 2 sunny lines left to cover these points. So we need to show that 2 sunny lines cannot cover all points of the triangle T' = {(a,b) : a≥2, b≥2, a+b≤n}. Let's denote m = n-2? The points in T' have coordinates between 2 and n-2. Thus, k=4 is impossible for n=4. For larger n, similar reasoning: the number of hypotenuse points is n. If we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k hypotenuse points. The remaining n-k hypotenuse points must be covered by non-sunny lines. If we don't use the hypotenuse line, we need at least n-k non-sunny lines to cover those points, each covering at most one (since they are not the hypotenuse line). So the total number of non-sunny lines must be at least n-k. But we have n-k lines non-sunny? Actually, total lines = n, sunny = k, non-sunny = n-k. So if we don't use the hypotenuse line, we need n-k non-sunny lines to cover n-k hypotenuse points, but each non-sunny line can cover at most one hypotenuse point, so we need at least n-k non-sunny lines, which is exactly the number we have. So that is possible if we have exactly n-k non-sunny lines and we use them to cover the remaining hypotenuse points, each covering one. That would require that all non-sunny lines are either horizontal or vertical and each contains exactly one hypotenuse point. That is possible if we choose the non-sunny lines to be, for example, y = 1 (covers (1,1) only? Actually, (1,1) is not a hypotenuse point because hypotenuse points have a+b=n+1, so (1,1) sum=2, not. So a horizontal line y=c covers only the hypotenuse point (n+1-c, c) if that point exists. So if we use horizontal lines y=1,...,n-1, each covers one hypotenuse point (except possibly the top). Similarly, vertical lines x=c cover one hypotenuse point (c, n+1-c). So it's possible to cover all hypotenuse points with n-1 non-sunny lines (if we use, say, y=1,...,n-1, that covers (n,1), (n-1,2), ..., (1,n) — that's all n points). So we could have non-sunny lines that are horizontal lines covering all hypotenuse points. But then we have no sunny lines? Wait, if we have k sunny lines, we have n-k non-sunny lines. If we use them to cover the hypotenuse points, we need at least n non-sunny lines to cover all n hypotenuse points (if each covers one). But we have only n-k non-sunny lines. So to cover n hypotenuse points, we need n non-sunny lines if we don't use the hypotenuse line. So we must have k=0 if we don't use the hypotenuse line. But if k>0, we could use the hypotenuse line to cover all n points with one non-sunny line, and then the remaining non-sunny lines can be used for other points. So for k≥1, we must include the hypotenuse line as a non-sunny line. So the only way to have k sunny lines is to have the hypotenuse line as one of the non-sunny lines. Then the remaining n-1 lines are non-sunny, and we have k sunny lines. So the total number of non-sunny lines is n-k, but we already used 1 for the hypotenuse, so we have n-k-1 other non-sunny lines that must be either horizontal or vertical. Those can be used to cover some of the boundary points. Then the uncovered points are those not covered by the non-sunny lines. <solution>
We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- For any n≥3, there exist configurations with exactly 0, 1, or 3 sunny lines.
- For n≥3, there is no configuration with exactly 2 sunny lines.
- The answer is: the possible k are 0, 1, and 3.

But we need to give a rigorous proof. Let me attempt to write a complete solution.

**Step 1: Definitions and basic observations.**  
Let P = {(a,b) ∈ ℕ^2 : a+b ≤ n+1}. We need n distinct lines L_1,…,L_n such that every point of P lies on at least one line. A line is called sunny if its direction is not parallel to the x‑axis, y‑axis, or the line x+y=0.

**Step 2: A lower bound on the number of sunny lines.**  
Consider the set of points on the hypotenuse H = {(1,n),(2,n-1),…,(n,1)}. There are n points.  
- A sunny line can contain at most one point of H (if it contained two, the line would be the hypotenuse, which is not sunny).  
- A non‑sunny line can contain at most one point of H unless it is exactly the line x+y=n+1, which contains all n points.

If we do not use the line x+y=n+1 as one of the lines, then each of the n lines can cover at most one point of H, so we would need at least n lines to cover H. Since we have exactly n lines, the only possibility is that all n lines are non‑sunny and each covers exactly one point of H. That would give s=0 sunny lines.  
If we use the line x+y=n+1 as one of the lines, it covers all n points of H. Then the remaining n-1 lines are non‑sunny (because any sunny line would cover a point of H, but we already have all H points covered; we may still have sunny lines, but they cannot cover any new H points). So the number of sunny lines can be any number from 0 to n-1, but we must also cover the points not on the hypotenuse.

**Step 3: The case s=2 is impossible.**  
Assume s=2, so exactly two lines are sunny. Then the remaining n-2 lines are non‑sunny. As argued, we must have the line x+y=n+1 among them; otherwise we would need n non‑sunny lines to cover H, but we only have n-2. So let L_0 = x+y=n+1 be one of the lines.

Now consider the left column L = {(1,1),…,(1,n)}. There are n points.  
- The line x=1 is vertical and would cover all n points of L.  
- A horizontal line y=c covers exactly one point of L, namely (1,c).  
- A sunny line can cover at most one point of L (because it is not vertical).  
- The line L_0 covers (1,n) only.

To cover all n points of L, we need either x=1 as a non‑sunny line, or enough horizontal lines to cover the rest. Since we have only n-3 non‑sunny lines besides L_0, we could use at most n-3 horizontal lines to cover n-3 points of L. The remaining two points (including (1,n) already covered by L_0) must be covered by the two sunny lines. That is possible. So we cannot rule out the left column by a simple count.

However, we can also consider the bottom row B = {(1,1),…,(n,1)}. Similar reasoning holds.

Now we need to cover the points not on the boundary. The set of points with a≥2,b≥2,a+b ≤ n is a triangle of size (n-2)(n-1)/2? Actually, points with a,b≥2 and a+b ≤ n+1: if a+b=n+1, those points are on the hypotenuse, already covered by L_0. So the points with a,b≥2 and a+b ≤ n are a triangle of size (n-2)(n-1)/2? Let’s compute: for b=2, a from 2 to n-2 gives n-3 points; b=3, a from 2 to n-3 gives n-4; …; b=n-2, a=2 gives 1 point; total = (n-3)+(n-4)+…+1 = (n-3)(n-2)/2. So the number of points in this triangle is T = (n-3)(n-2)/2. For n≥5, T ≥ 3.

Now, after using L_0 and the n-3 other non‑sunny lines (horizontal/vertical), we need to cover the points not on L_0, nor on those n-3 lines. The uncovered points will be a subset of the triangle. Let us denote by U the set of points of P that are not on any of the n-2 non‑sunny lines. Since the n-3 lines besides L_0 are either horizontal or vertical, each such line can cover many points. The uncovered set U must be covered by the two sunny lines.

We need to show that no matter how we choose the n-3 horizontal/vertical lines, the set U always contains at least 3 points that cannot be covered by two sunny lines. More precisely, we will prove that the two sunny lines can cover at most 2 points from the triangle of points with a,b≥2,a+b ≤ n, unless one of them is the line x=1 or y=1? But we already used those possibly.

**Step 4: A counting argument.**  
Let us examine the structure of the uncovered set. The points of the triangle are arranged in rows b=2,3,…,n-2. For each b, the points are (2,b),(3,b),…,(n-b,b). Consider the total number of points in the triangle: T = (n-3)(n-2)/2. Now, each of the n-3 non‑sunny lines (besides L_0) can be either horizontal or vertical.  
- A horizontal line y=c covers the points with that b=c, but only those with a≥2 and a≤n-c? Actually, it covers the whole row b=c for a=1..n+1-c; but the points with a=1 are on the left column, which we may have covered. For the triangle, a≥2, so a horizontal line y=c covers at most n-c points from the triangle (if c≤n-2).  
- A vertical line x=c covers at most n+1-c points from the triangle (if c≥2).  

The total number of points in the triangle that can be covered by the n-3 lines is at most the sum of the numbers of points each line covers. But we cannot simply sum because the lines can overlap. However, we can bound the number of uncovered points from below by using a double counting: the total number of incidences between the triangle points and the n-3 lines is at most (n-3)*max coverage per line. The maximum coverage of a line in the triangle is n-2 (for y=1? Actually, y=1 is not in triangle; for triangle, the horizontal line y=2 covers a from 2 to n-2, so n-3 points; the vertical line x=2 covers a=2? Actually, vertical x=2 covers points with b from 2 to n-1? but triangle requires b≤n-2, so b from 2 to n-2, gives n-3 points. So each line can cover at most n-3 points from the triangle. Thus, the total number of triangle points covered by the n-3 lines is at most (n-3)*(n-3). But that is a very crude bound; many points may be covered multiple times, so the number of uncovered points can be as high as T. To make the uncovered set small, we must choose the n-3 lines to cover most triangle points. In the construction with k=3, we used three lines: x=1, y=1, and the hypotenuse, which are non‑sunny, leaving a triangle of 6 points (for n=6, the triangle of points with a,b≥2,a+b≤6 has 6 points). Those 6 points are then covered by the 3 sunny lines. So for s=2, we would have only 2 sunny lines to cover the triangle points. The triangle has T points. To cover all T points with 2 sunny lines, we need each sunny line to cover at least T/2 points. But can a sunny line cover many points from the triangle? In the triangle of points with a,b≥2,a+b≤n, a sunny line (slope not 0,∞,-1) can contain at most 2 points? Actually, we need to check. For n=6, the triangle has 6 points: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). A sunny line can contain at most 2 of them (e.g., y=x contains (2,2),(3,3); y=-2x+8 contains (2,4),(3,2); y=-0.5x+4 contains (2,3),(4,2)). So each covers 2 points. With 2 lines, max 4 points, leaving 2 uncovered. So impossible. For n=7, the triangle has 10 points: (2,2)-(2,5); (3,2)-(3,4); (4,2)-(4,3); (5,2). A sunny line can contain at most 3 points? Check y=x: contains (2,2),(3,3) but (4,4) not in triangle (sum=8>7). So 2 points. y=x+1: (2,3),(3,4) — 2 points. y=x+2: (2,4) only — 1 point. y=-0.5x+? Through (2,5) and (5,2) slope -1, not; through (2,3) and (4,2) slope -0.5? (2,3) and (4,2): slope -0.5, line y=-0.5x+4? Check: (2,3): -1+4=3; (4,2): -2+4=2; so that's 2 points. So max 2 points. Thus, with 2 lines, max 4 points, leaving 6 uncovered. So impossible. For n=8, the triangle has 15 points. A sunny line can contain up to 3 points (e.g., y=x contains (2,2),(3,3),(4,4) because (4,4) sum=8, so it's in triangle). So 3 points. With 2 lines, max 6 points, leaving 9 uncovered. So impossible. For n=9, the triangle has 21 points. A sunny line can contain up to 4 points? y=x: (2,2),(3,3),(4,4),(5,5) but (5,5) sum=10>9? Actually, a+b≤9, so (5,5) sum=10>9, not in triangle; so only (2,2),(3,3),(4,4) — 3 points. y=x+1: (2,3),(3,4),(4,5) but (4,5) sum=9, yes; so 3 points. So max 3 points. So with 2 lines, max 6 points, leaving 15 uncovered. So impossible.

Thus, for n≥5, the triangle has at least 3 points, and the two sunny lines can cover at most 2 * floor((n-2)/2?) Actually, the maximum number of points a sunny line can contain from the triangle is at most something like: for n, the triangle is points with a,b≥2, a+b≤n. The number of points on the diagonal a=b is at most floor((n-1)/2) - 1? For n=9, points on a=b: (2,2),(3,3),(4,4) — 3 points; that's the maximum for that line. So the max coverage per sunny line is about n/2? But for n=9, max is 3, which is less than n/2. So it's not that many. In general, the triangle has T points, and the maximum number of points on a sunny line is at most floor((n-1)/2) - 1? Actually, for n=8, max is 3; for n=7, max is 2; for n=6, max is 2. So the max is less than T/2 for n≥6? T = (n-3)(n-2)/2. For n=8, T=15, max=3, so T/2=7.5, 3<7.5. For n=9, T=21, max=3, 3<10.5. So the two sunny lines can cover at most 2*max < T for n≥6. Thus, they cannot cover all T points. So it is impossible to cover the triangle with 2 sunny lines. Therefore, s=2 is impossible.

**Step 5: The case s=0,1,3 are possible.**  
We already gave constructions:  
- s=0: take the n horizontal lines y=1,2,…,n. They cover all points.  
- s=1: for any n≥3, we can use the non‑sunny lines x=1, y=1, y=2, …, y=n-2 (that’s n-2 lines) and the sunny line y=x+? Actually, we need to check that we can cover all points with exactly one sunny line. For n=4, we used x=1, y=1, y=2 as non‑sunny and y=x+1 as sunny. For n=5, we used x=1, y=1, y=2, x+y=6 as non‑sunny and y=x+1 as sunny. For n=6, we used x=1, y=1, y=2, y=3, x=2 as non‑sunny and y=x+1 as sunny. In general, we can take the non‑sunny lines to be: x=1, y=1, y=2, …, y=n-3, and also the hypotenuse x+y=n+1. That gives (n-2) lines? Actually, x=1 and y=1..n-3 gives n-2 lines; plus the hypotenuse gives n-1 lines. Then we have one line left to be sunny; we can take y=x+1 (or some other). But we need to ensure that the uncovered points after those non‑sunny lines are exactly the points that can be covered by the sunny line. The uncovered points will be a set of points that lie on a line that is sunny. By a systematic choice, we can always find such a configuration (e.g., by taking the non‑sunny lines to be all vertical/horizontal lines that cover the boundary, leaving a "staircase" that can be covered by a single sunny line). The details are straightforward but we can give explicit constructions:
   - For n≥4, use lines: x=1, y=1, y=2, …, y=n-3, and x+y=n+1. These are n-2 lines. Then the remaining points are those with a≥2,b≥n-2,a+b≤n. That set has exactly 2 points? Better to give a general explicit construction: take the n lines to be the n horizontal lines y=1,…,n (that gives k=0).
